<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="suggested-reading-229">SUGGESTED READING 229<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#suggested-reading-229" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="index-231-233">INDEX 231-233<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#index-231-233" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="preface">Preface<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preface" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the modern engineering curriculum, with the emphasis on engineering science, workshop and laboratory practices have acquired greater importance in the recent years. With major advancements in precise instrumentation and computerized manufacturing techniques, the same has become more sophisticated and specialized field. To gain a basic knowledge of manufacturing process, a student entering the first year of degree or diploma must undertake a course on workshop practice. This gives him/her an overall knowledge about various engineering materials, tools, equipment and processes which are common in the engineering field. Multiple requests from my students and colleagues for a good supporting textbook were my motivation to write the book <em>Mechanical Workshop Practice</em>.</p>
<p>The favourable and warm reception of first edition of the book “Mechanical Workshop Practice” gave me satisfaction and encouragement to bring out the second edition. I have utilized this opportunity to revise the book little and add one chapter on Plumbing.</p>
<p>This book consists of three modules. Module A is an introduction to workshop practice. Its first chapter explains general principles and layout of workshop processes such as primary and secondary shaping processes, metal joining methods, surface finishing, heat treatments and the materials for workpieces and tools. The second chapter describes the importance of safety measures to be followed as well as the procedure of writing the work and fair records of the practice.</p>
<p>Module B deals with the hand working processes such as fitting, arc welding, sheet metal work, carpentry, blacksmithy and foundry. Each chapter in this module initially explains the tools and equipment used and then the process. Drawings of models and the construction procedures are given at the end as examples.</p>
<p>The revised first year syllabus of many Indian Universities includes Plumbing as a new trade for practice. Hear the trade is added as Chapter 9 in the book under the Module B “Hand Working Processes”. Enough figures and examples have been added in the chapter in order to understand the topic clearly and to practice the trade. The method of sketching isometric single line piping layout is also added to prepare the details of plumbing.</p>
<p>Module C describes the machining processes and model making. The working principle of engine lathe and turning of cylindrical machine components are covered in this module. Each chapter of the book ends with exercises having enough questions for viva voce and drawings for making models. The process chart and the related tables for tolerances and fits are also given as an Appendix for advanced level practice and the assessment of work.</p>
<p>I am greatly indebted to many specialists on this subject, whose publications have contributed to my knowledge on the subject and to my teachers devoted to the engineering profession. I am especially thankful to Dr. K. Balakrishnan, Principal (Rtd.), Govt. Engineering College, Thrissur, who was kind enough to review the book and give his valuable suggestions for the betterment of the book. I express my deep gratitude to my colleagues who have directly and indirectly helped me to make this attempt a great success. My heartfelt thanks are also due to my son Simon K. John (M.Tech.) for preparing the drawings using CAD.</p>
<p>I sincerely thank the students and teaching community who have used the first edition of the book and gave me valuable suggestions for improvement. Finally I thank the PHI Learning group once again for their timely and careful processing of the second edition.</p>
<p>K.C. John</p>
<p>Module A</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="introduction-to-workshop-practice">Introduction to Workshop Practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction-to-workshop-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>1</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="workshop-processes">Workshop Processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#workshop-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The workshop technology gives the basic working knowledge for the production of various engineering products. It explains the construction, function and the use of various working tools, measuring tools, equipment and machines as well as the technique of manufacturing a product from its raw material. To understand the subject and to develop the skill, one has to practice the workshop processes, starting from simple models to complicated products.</p>
<p>Many of the aspects of workshop technique can only be acquired by experience, by studying on the spot and by establishing contacts with experienced craftsmen. There is much, however, that may be learned by reading prior to or along with the workshop practice. The objective of this part of the book is to provide a preliminary insight into the subject for easy understanding and to give direction for fast progress in the practice. It is to be noted that the basic knowledge of composition, properties and use of different materials, fundamentals about the process of manufacturing, use of hand tools and operations, and allied techniques converts an ordinary student of science into an engineer. Also, the basic knowledge and experience in workshop processes facilitate a sound foundation for further advanced engineering studies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-1-classification-of-manufacturing-processes">1.1 Classification of Manufacturing Processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-classification-of-manufacturing-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Metal is considered as the most important material for engineering objects and machines. The term, manufacturing process is considered as the general name for the method of processing metal in order to obtain various shapes. The whole manufacturing processes can be broadly classified into the following five groups:</p>
<ol>
<li>Primary shaping processes</li>
<li>Secondary shaping (machining) processes</li>
<li>Joining processes</li>
<li>Surface finishing processes</li>
<li>Processes changing the metal properties</li>
</ol>
<p>The above-mentioned manufacturing processes can also be grouped as hot working and cold-working processes. In hot-working process, the metal is processed in hot condition, i.e., above the recrystallization temperature, whereas in cold-working process, the processing is done below this temperature.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-2-primary-shaping-processes">1.2 Primary Shaping Processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-primary-shaping-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Metal obtained by extraction of ores is refined and cast to form big blocks, called ingots. These ingots are reduced to smaller size and shape by one or more of the primary shaping processes. A list of the important primary shaping processes is given below.</p>
<ol>
<li>Metal casting</li>
<li>Rolling</li>
<li>Forging</li>
<li>Extrusion</li>
<li>Metal drawing</li>
<li>Powder metallurgy</li>
<li>Oxy-gas cutting</li>
<li>Press work</li>
<li>Sheet metal work</li>
<li>Metal spinning</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-2-1-metal-casting">1.2.1 Metal Casting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-1-metal-casting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Casting or founding is the process of forming metal objects by melting and pouring into moulds. A foundry is a commercial establishment for founding or producing metal castings. Metal casting can be broadly divided into types:</p>
<ol>
<li>Conventional sand casting process.</li>
<li>Special casting processes.</li>
</ol>
<p>The best known and still widely used method of metal casting is the conventional sand casting process. Sand casting consists basically of pouring molten metal into appropriate cavities, formed in a sand mould. Whichever may be the metal poured into the sand moulds, the product is called sand casting. The process of sand casting includes the following operations:</p>
<ol>
<li>Pattern making</li>
<li>Core making</li>
<li>Moulding</li>
<li>Melting and pouring</li>
<li>Fettling and cleaning</li>
<li>Testing and inspection</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-96bb8f7f.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg">
Figure 1.1 shows an example to sand mould used for casting a single component. The sand casting methods can also be grouped according to the metal cast as shown herein below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Grey iron castings</li>
<li>Steel castings</li>
<li>Nonferrous castings
FIGURE 1.1 Sand mould with casting.</li>
</ol>
<p>The metal casting methods other than the conventional sand casting process are grouped as special casting processes. They include the following:</p>
<ol>
<li>Shell mould casting</li>
<li>Investment mould casting</li>
<li>Permanent mould casting</li>
<li>Die casting</li>
<li>Slush casting</li>
<li>Plaster mould casting</li>
<li>Centrifugal casting</li>
<li>Continuous casting</li>
</ol>
<p>Foundry practice is explained in Chapter 8.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-2-2-rolling">1.2.2 Rolling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-2-rolling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Rolling is a process of shaping metals and alloys into semi-finished products by passing them between two rolls rotating in opposite directions [Figure 1.2(a)]. Rolling involves plastic deformation of the metal in which the thickness of the outgoing metal is reduced and the length is increased. The rolling processes can be broadly divided into two sections as mentioned below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Breaking down processes: to produce semi-finished products called blooms, slabs, and billets from the heavy initial metal block called ingot.</li>
<li>Finish rolling processes: to produce rails, beams, plates, sheets, structural sections, rods, etc., from the blooms, slabs and billets.</li>
</ol>
<p>The rolling process is basically a hot-working process but, thin sheets and foils are rolled in clod (at room temperature) state. Cross sections of some commonly used rolled products are shown in Figure 1.2(b). These products are used as the basic raw material for further shaping processes like welding, forging, sheet metal work, machining, etc.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-ee84e801.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
(a) Rolling process
FIGURE 1.2 Rolling.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-7c67576f.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
(b) Rolled sections</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-3-forging">1.2.3 Forging<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-3-forging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Forging is basically a hot-working process of metal, performed by means of hammer blows or the pressure applied by a press. Various types of machine parts of different shapes and sizes are made by forging or stamping operations. Figure 1.3 shows the principle of open die forging. Forging operation can be done by hand or by using machines. Depending on the method of production, forging processes are classified into the following types:</p>
<ol>
<li>Hand forging</li>
<li>Power hammer forging</li>
<li>Drop forging or closed die forging</li>
<li>Press forging</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-0bb2b451.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.3 Open die forging.</p>
<p>Hand forging is carried out on an anvil with the aid of hand forging tools. Heavy components are forged by using power hammers. To change the shape and control the dimensions of a workpiece, various blacksmith's tools are used. Shaping of metal with the help of hand hammers or power hammers is called smith forging. In drop forging or stamping, the flow of metal is limited by the space of the recess in the dies, in which the metal takes its predetermined shape and dimensions. Every part manufactured by drop-forging process requires a separate die or a set of dies. Very heavy components are forged by squeezing the hot metal between dies using presses. Such forging is called press forging. Here, instead of suddenly applying the force as a blow, the force is applied gradually like squeezing.</p>
<p>The forging operation is associated with a change in the microstructure of the metal that leads to a rearrangement of fibres and alters the grain size. The layout of grains of a crank shaft made by casting, machining from a solid piece and forging is shown in Figure 1.4 for comparison. The forging process gives the best fibre grain flow layout resulting better strength against dynamic loads. Parts like crank shafts, connecting rods, machine livers, spanners, etc., are manufactured by the drop-forging process. The hand-forging process is explained in Chapter 7.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-53650b9f.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.4 Grain flow lines in a crank shaft.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-2-4-extrusion">1.2.4 Extrusion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-4-extrusion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Extrusion is the process of forcing metal enclosed in a container and forming the shape by passing out through the opening of a die. The cross-section of the extruded metal is that of the contour and dimension of the opening of the die. Extrusion process is very well used to produce nonferrous metal sections. The initial material for extrusion is cast or rolled billets. Figure 1.5 explains the principle of extrusion. Extrusion processes can be classified as:</p>
<ol>
<li>Direct extrusion</li>
<li>Indirect extrusion</li>
</ol>
<p>In the direct extrusion, both the ram and the extruded metal move in the same direction as shown in Figure 1.5 while in indirect extrusion they move in opposite directions. The extrusion process can be done in both hot or cold state of metal. Long sections of various shapes and tubes can be manufactured by the direct extrusion process. For the extrusion of tubes, a mandrel is pierced first through the billet and then they are extruded together through the die, producing the tube. Extrusion of aluminium is a very commonly used process to produce material for doors and windows, furniture, automobile body, etc.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-a44d57b0.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.5 Extrusion (direct).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-2-5-metal-drawing">1.2.5 Metal Drawing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-5-metal-drawing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Metal drawing is a process in which wire, bar stock or tube is drawn in tension through the hole of a drawing die. The hole has a conical shape built in such a way that the inlet side is slightly larger and the outlet side is slightly smaller than the object drawn. Figure 1.6 explains the principle of wire drawing.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-3b4e1fd9.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.6 Wire drawing.</p>
<p>The drawing process is usually done in the cold state and is applied to nonferrous metals and low-carbon steels. The operation is done on powerful drawing benches with high pulling capacity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-2-6-powder-metallurgy">1.2.6 Powder Metallurgy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-6-powder-metallurgy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Powder metallurgy can be described as an art of manufacturing commercial articles from powdered metals by placing the powder in moulds and applying pressure. These compressed components are then heated to bind the particles together to get improved strength and other properties. This process is known as sintering. In sintering the</p>
<p>temperature is kept below the melting point of the powder. Powder metallurgy enables the production of alloys from both metallic and nonmetallic powders. This enables to produce parts which are having unusual characteristics. A few examples of such products are cemented carbide tools, oil-less bearings, special electrical contacts, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-7-oxy-gas-cutting">1.2.7 Oxy-Gas Cutting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-7-oxy-gas-cutting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The oxy-gas cutting is based upon the ability of certain metals to burn in the presence of oxygen, expelling heavy heat to melt and form oxides. The metals which are having melting points above the ignition temperature of the metal, form oxides. Carbon steels with carbon percentage up to 0.7 and low alloy steel can be cut by this process. The oxy-gas cutting is performed by means of ordinary gas welding equipment except that the welding torch is replaced by the cutting torch. A gas mixture for pre-heating (oxy-acetyline) is passed through the outer ring-holes of the nozzle, while the cutting oxygen at higher pressure is delivered through the central hole. The oxy-gas cutting can be manual, semiautomatic or automatic. The mechanization of cutting process enables more uniform motion of the torch resulting good quality cut at higher speeds. Oxy-gas cutting process is very well used in structural steel industries to shape metal for welding, fabrication and allied processes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-2-8-press-work">1.2.8 Press Work<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-8-press-work" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Press work is a term applied to a large variety of operations in connection with the metallic and nonmetallic articles. The operations are done generally in cold state. The press work operations can be broadly grouped as shearing and forming processes (Figure 1.7).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-7ed47cdd.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.7 Shearing and forming operations in press work.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="shearing-operations">Shearing operations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shearing-operations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>(a) Blanking: This is the process of cutting a closed outside contour of a shape.
(b) Piercing: This is the process of cutting holes of any shape or size inside the contour.
(c) Perforating: This is the process of cutting a number of small holes inside the contour.
(d) Notching: This is the cutting of relatively small indentation on the edge of a part.
(e) Trimming: This is a process of cutting of the excess metal from a piece.
(f) Shaving: This is a secondary cutting operation in which the surface of a previously cut edge is finished for bringing close dimensional accuracy.
(g) Slitting: This is a process of making incomplete cut in a sheet so that a projected portion is obtained.
(h) Lancing: This is the process of cutting along a line in the workpiece, without producing a detached portion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="forming-operations">Forming operations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#forming-operations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>(a) Bending: This is a process of forming bends in one plain only.
(b) Forming: This is a process of bending into complicated shapes in more than one plane.
(c) Curling: This is a process of shaping an edge to circular cross section along the edge of a sheet or at the end of a shell or tube.
(d) Wiring: This is a curling process in which a wire is inserted within the curled edge.
(e) Embossing: This is a forming process in which projections like letters, designs etc. are made on a thin metal sheet.
(f) Coining: This is a forming process by which the surface is deformed into fine detail by compression.</p>
<p>(g) Drawing: This is a forming process by which a flat blank is converted into a cup or a cylindrical form with one end closed.</p>
<p>The press used for shearing or forming may be hand operated, foot operated, hydraulic operated, air operated or power operated. For light works, hand presses are sufficient. For cutting and forming processes, dies and punches of hard steel material are used. The finish and accuracy of object formed by press work depends mainly on the size, shape, dimension and material of die blocks. The types of articles produced by press work range from a small flat blank to a large one roof panel of a saloon car. The size of the object is limited by the size of the die block and the capacity of the power press. In modern times, most of the sheet metal parts of small machines like washing machine, typewriter, electronic equipment, steel furniture, cycle parts, electrical fittings, automobile body parts, etc., are produced by various press-working operations.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-2-9-sheet-metal-work">1.2.9 Sheet Metal Work<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-9-sheet-metal-work" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sheet metal work is basically a hand process of making objects of sheets of metal with the help of hand tools and equipment. The object to be made is developed and drawn on a plane sheet of metal. Then it is cut and folded to form the shape. The joints are made by folding, riveting or soldering.</p>
<p>Ferrous, nonferrous and coated metallic sheets are used for the production of the sheet-metal objects. Machines like shearing machine, folding machine, bending machine etc. are also used for large-scale production of sheet metal objects. Examples for sheet metal works are hoppers, trays, guards, covers, funnel, bucket, measuring can, hoods, boxes, pipes, etc. Sheet metal work is explained in Chapter 5.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-2-10-metal-spinning">1.2.10 Metal Spinning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-10-metal-spinning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Metal spinning is a forming process in which the metal sheet is rotated on a chuck or former or die. The deformation of the metal sheet is obtained by a combination of bending and stretching due to the application of pressure using the forming tool (see Figure 1.8).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-87f5eadc.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.8 Principle of spinning operation.</p>
<p>The spinning process can be done in both hot and cold state of metal. Hot spinning is used to produce large and heavy objects like pressure vessels, cylindrical tank ends, etc. The machine used for the process is called spinning lathe. The metals that are available in sheet form, can be spun. Hand spinning is successfully done in many small-scale industries to produce aluminium utensils. The objects produced by spinning includes conical shapes, utensils, ornaments, components used for chemical industries, pressure vessel cylinder ends, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-3-secondary-shaping-processes">1.3 Secondary Shaping Processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-3-secondary-shaping-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The components or parts produced by primary shaping processes may not have the required accuracy in shape, dimension and surface finish due to the limitations of the process used. A secondary shaping process called machining is applied on these components for obtaining the desired finish. Machining is defined as metal-cutting process in which metal is removed from a surface in the form of chips using a tool. A list of the commonly used machining processes is given below.</p>
<ol>
<li>Turning</li>
<li>Drilling</li>
<li>Shaping</li>
<li>Planing</li>
<li>Slotting</li>
<li>Milling</li>
<li>Grinding</li>
<li>Boring</li>
<li>Broaching</li>
<li>Hobbing</li>
<li>Unconventional machining processes.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="turning">Turning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#turning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Turning is a metal-cutting process performed in a machine tool called lathe. Here, the workpiece clamped on a spindle is rotated and the tool is pressed against the moving surface to remove the metal [Figure 1.9(a)]. Turning is the most common machining process used to produce cylindrical objects. More about turning is discussed in Chapter 9.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="drilling">Drilling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drilling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Drilling is a metal cutting process in which holes are produced by pressing a rotating cylindrical tool called twist drill against a stationary workpiece. The machine used for this is called drilling machine [Figure 1.9(b)]. Drilling is the simplest conventional machining process and is discussed in Chapter 3.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="shaping">Shaping<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shaping" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Shaping is a metal-cutting process in which plane surfaces or grooves are produced by horizontal reciprocating motion of a tool against a stationary workpiece [see Fig. 1.9(c)]. Shaping is done in a machine tool called shaper and the process is suitable to produce plane surfaces or grooves of relatively short lengths on small or medium-size objects.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="planing">Planing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#planing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Planing is a metal-cutting process in which plane surfaces or grooves are produced by horizontal reciprocating motion of the workpiece against a stationary tool [Figure 1.9(d)]. Planing is done in a machine tool called planer and the process is used to produce plane surfaces or grooves of relatively large objects.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="slotting">Slotting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#slotting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Slotting is a metal-cutting process in which slots or grooves like keyways are produced by vertical reciprocating motion of the tool against a stationary workpiece [Figure 1.9(e)]. Slotting is done in a machine tool called slotting machine and the process is generally used to cut keyways or grooves inside holes.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-207e05c1.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg">
(a) Turning</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-bb828bb2.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg">
(b) Drilling</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-8cb77d13.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg">
(c) Shaping</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-f39d6394.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg">
(d) Planing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-8ab0851e.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg">
(e) Slotting</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-c64f957c.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg">
(f) Milling</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-132da6bd.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.9 Secondary shaping (machining) processes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="milling">Milling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#milling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Milling is a metal-cutting process in which a rotating disc or cylindrical-shaped multiple point cutter is brought against a horizontal moving workpiece. Almost all types of geometrical surfaces with better accuracy and finish can be produced by this process [Figure 1.9(f)]. Milling is done in a machine tool called milling machine and the process is commonly used to make very accurate machine-parts having complicated shapes with good surface finish.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="grinding">Grinding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#grinding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Grinding is a metal-cutting operation in which metal is removed in the form of fine chips by means of an abrasive wheel rotating at high speed [Figure 1.9(g)]. Grinding is done in a machine tool called grinding machine and the process is used to get high surface finish, accuracy of shape and dimension.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="boring">Boring<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#boring" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Boring is a metal-cutting process like turning in which the large-sized cylindrical holes are machined. Here the tool fixed on a boring bar, which is supported at two ends, rotates inside the stationary workpiece to affect machining [Figure 1.9(h)]. This boring process is conducted in machines similar to lathe called boring machines.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="broaching">Broaching<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#broaching" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Broaching is also a metal-cutting process in which a typical cutter called broach is drawn or pulled against a surface in order to cut a hole or groove. Broaching is commonly used to produce components having special-shaped holes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="hobbing">Hobbing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hobbing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Hobbing is another metal-cutting process in which a cutter called hob revolves and removes metal from the workpiece like milling process. Hobbing is commonly used process to cut large-sized gears.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="unconventional-machining-processes">Unconventional machining processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unconventional-machining-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The unconventional or non-traditional machining processes are the metal-removing processes other than the above-explained metal-cutting processes. In these processes, the object is shaped by removing metal, using some form of energy. A list of popular unconventional machining processes is given below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Ultrasonic machining (U.S.M.)</li>
<li>Abrasive jet machining (A.J.M.)</li>
<li>Electro-chemical machining (E.C.M.)</li>
<li>Plasma arc machining (P.A.M.)</li>
<li>Electrical discharge machining (E.D.M.)</li>
<li>Electron beam machining (E.B.M.)</li>
<li>Laser beam machining (L.B.M.)</li>
</ol>
<p>Unconventional machining processes are economical and have wide application when the metal machined is very hard or is of special alloy. Intricate shapes of dies used for injection moulding are produced by these processes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-4-joining-processes">1.4 Joining Processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-joining-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Different metallic pieces are joined together to get the required shape or assembled form of parts. The joints, produced may be of permanent or temporary nature. The different joining processes used in workshop practice are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Welding</li>
<li>Brazing and soldering</li>
<li>Screwing</li>
<li>Riveting</li>
<li>Shrink fitting</li>
<li>Adhesive bonding</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-4-1-welding">1.4.1 Welding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-1-welding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Welding is the process of joining metallic parts by bringing them into intimate proximity and heating the places of contact to the state of fusion or plasticity. This leads to inter penetration of atoms of the metals in the weld zone and a strong inseparable joint is formed after the metals have cooled. The popular welding processes are mentioned below:</p>
<p>(a) Arc welding</p>
<ol>
<li>Shielded metal arc welding</li>
<li>Submerged arc welding</li>
<li>Gas-metal-arc (MIG) welding</li>
<li>Tungsten-arc (TIG) welding</li>
<li>Automatic hydrogen welding</li>
<li>Flux coated arc welding</li>
<li>Carbon arc welding</li>
<li>Electro-slag welding</li>
<li>Stud welding.</li>
</ol>
<p>(b) Resistance welding</p>
<ol>
<li>Spot welding</li>
<li>Seam welding</li>
<li>Projection welding</li>
<li>Butt and upset welding</li>
<li>Flash welding</li>
<li>Percussion welding.</li>
</ol>
<p>(c) Gas welding</p>
<ol>
<li>Oxy-acetylene welding</li>
<li>Oxy-hydrogen welding</li>
<li>Air-acetylene welding</li>
<li>Oxy-other fuel gas welding.</li>
</ol>
<p>(d) Miscellaneous welding processes</p>
<ol>
<li>Thermit welding</li>
<li>Forge welding</li>
<li>Modern welding processes.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="arc-welding">Arc welding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#arc-welding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Among the various arc welding processes, shielded metal arc welding (manual metal arc welding) is the most widely used and less expensive welding process. Shielded metal arc welding is a manual arc welding</p>
<p>process in fusion state, in which the heat required for melting the surfaces to be joined is generated by maintaining an electric arc between a flux-coated consumable electrode and the base metal. More about the process is explained in Chapter 4. Submerged arc welding is also popular in industries due to its suitability for welding thick metals. The remaining arc welding processes given above are generally selected to join metals which are difficult to weld or when special requirements are existing. Figure 1.10(a) shows examples of arc-welded joints. Since the shielded metal arc welding (manual metal arc welding) is so popular, less expensive and suitable to weld mild steel of reasonable thickness, the process is also called in short form arc welding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="resistance-welding">Resistance welding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#resistance-welding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In resistance welding, the heat for joining is obtained by resistance offered by the work-pieces to the flow of electric current in a circuit in which the work is a part. The most common resistance welding processes are spot, seam and projection welding. Sheet metal components, wire meshes, welded pipes, etc. are produced by this process. Figure 1.10(b) shows the examples for resistance welded joints.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-d3b63150.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg">
(a) Welding in fusion state (arc or gas welding)
FIGURE 1.10 Welded joints.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-4fe9cbd0.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg">
(b) Welding in plastic state (resistance welding)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="gas-welding">Gas welding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#gas-welding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In gas welding, the heat generated by the combustion of various gases in a stream of oxygen is used to melt the welding area and filler metal. The most popular gas-welding process is oxy-acetylene welding. This is a fusion welding process used to join thin metallic sheets or pieces. Since the oxy-acetylene welding is very popular, this method of welding is also known as gas welding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="miscellaneous-welding-processes">Miscellaneous welding processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#miscellaneous-welding-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Other than the above-explained groups of processes, there are other welding processes which are used for special situations, joints, metals, etc. Thermit welding, for example, makes use of the heat supplied by an exo-thermic reaction involving combustion of thermit which is a mixture of powdered aluminium and iron oxide. This process is used to join rails in railway tracks. Forge welding is the oldest form of welding process done in blacksmith shop. In this process, the work-pieces are heated to plastic state and the superimposing ends are hammered to get the joint. The modern welding processes developed to join metals, which are difficult to weld or the welding conditions, size, etc., are not suitable for conventional processes. These processes include electron beam welding, laser welding, plasma arc welding, ultrasonic welding, explosive welding, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-4-2-brazing-and-soldering">1.4.2 Brazing and Soldering<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-2-brazing-and-soldering" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Brazing and soldering are the processes for joining solid metal components similar to welding by introducing a molten alloy between them which has a low melting point than the base metal. Brazing and soldering are used to join ferrous and nonferrous metals and their alloys.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="brazing">Brazing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#brazing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Brazing is a metal joining process in which the joint is produced by heating the base metal pieces to a suitable temperature above  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>430</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">430^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">43</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>  and applying a filler metal (brazing alloy) which has melting point below that of the base metal. This filler metal is distributed between closely-fitted surfaces of joint by capillary action. Figure 1.11 gives an example for the brazed joint. Brazing process is classified according to the type of heating of the base metal as mentioned below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Furnace brazing.</li>
<li>Torch brazing.</li>
<li>Induction brazing.</li>
<li>Salt-bath brazing.</li>
<li>Infra-red brazing.</li>
<li>Electric block brazing.</li>
</ol>
<p>The commonly used filler metals are the alloys of copper, silver, aluminium, magnesium and nickel. The flux for brazing is selected</p>
<p>according to the type of brazing alloy used. They are used to prevent oxidation, to dissolve oxides and to promote capillarity of the filler metal.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-3ac7e2dd.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.11 Braced point.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="soldering">Soldering<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#soldering" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Soldering is defined as a metallurgical bonding method in which a filler metal has a melting point below <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>430</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">430^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">43</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span>. Most engineering metals such as iron, steel, copper, brass, etc. can be joined by this process. Soldering is extensively used to make electrical and electronic connections, hermetic sealing of metal containers, joining of copper tubing, etc. Figure 1.12 gives a typical joint made by soldering. The soldering metal is basically an alloy of tin and lead of standard proportion of 40:60 by weight. Flux is to be applied at the joining area for making the joint.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-632413ed.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.12 Soldered joint.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-4-3-screwing">1.4.3 Screwing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-3-screwing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Screwed joints are temporary joints made by using threaded fasteners like bolts, nuts, screws, studs, etc. These joints are very common for assembly of parts in machines and equipment. The threads provided on the screwed fasteners join the parts together, transmit power and locate the parts in relation to each other. Figure 1.13 shows a typical screwed joint. A screw thread can be described as a helical or spiral groove or ridge formed on a cylindrical surface, such that the angular displacement of any two corresponding points on the counter of the groove is proportional to their displacement along the axis of the thread. The thread form cut externally on a male member such as, bolt, stud, etc., is known as external thread. The thread form cut internally on a female member like nut, socket, etc., is known as internal thread. To make a screwed joint, the internal and external thread forms should match each other. The helical form of thread can be of right-hand or left-hand type. In the case of a right-hand thread, a bolt head or nut is rotated clockwise, it moves forward to tighten the joint. For left-hand type, the opposite action occurs. Most of the threaded fasteners are of right-hand type. Left-hand threads are not common and used only for special purposes. The most common thread forms in use are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>British Standard Withworth (B.S.W.) threads in F.P.S. system</li>
<li>I.S.O. thread in metric system</li>
<li>Square thread for heavily loaded parts</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-b7728d99.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.13 Screwed joint.</p>
<p>The types of threaded fasteners used to make joints, include the following items:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Bolts
(i) Hexagonal headed bolt
(ii) Square-headed bolt
(iii) Round or cup headed bolt
(iv) T-headed bolt
(v) Hook bolt
(vi) Eye bolt</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nuts
(i) Hexagonal nut
(ii) Square nut
(iii) Wing nut
(iv) Knurled nut
(v) Castle nut
(vi) Slotted nut</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Studs
(i) Plain stud
(ii) Stud with square central part</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>(iii) Stud with collar</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>Screws
(i) Screws with different forms of heads.
(ii) Allen screw
(iii) Machine screw
(iv) Set screw
(v) Screw without head</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Washers
(i) Standard washer
(ii) Spring washer
(iii) Lock washer</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>When the screwed joints are subjected to vibrations, they have a tendency to get loose and screw off. The machine parts which are subjected to frequent vibration are joined by nuts with locking arrangements. Nuts with screws and spilt pin or lock washers are used for locking the nut. Usually a washer is inserted between the nut or head of bolt and the surface on which it is tightened. This enables easy tightening of nut or bolt and better distribution of clamping pressure on the machine body. The tools used to tighten and loosen the threaded fasteners are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>C-spanner</li>
<li>Ring spanner</li>
<li>Box spanner</li>
<li>Adjustable spanner</li>
<li>Screw driver</li>
<li>Allen key</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-4-4-riveting">1.4.4 Riveting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-4-riveting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Riveting is a permanent fastening method to join two or more pieces of plates or sheets. Riveting is done by using a cylindrical metal piece called rivet. It consists of a cylindrical body and a head. To make a joint by riveting, holes of diameter equal to that of the body of rivet is made on the plates in the joining position and the rivet body is inserted through the hole. The projecting tail-end is deformed to form the second head by the application of pressure using riveting tool or by hammering. Figure 1.14 shows a typical riveted joint. The rivets can be of solid or hollow type. They are grouped according to the shape of head. The common riveted joints are mentioned below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Lap joint.</li>
<li>Butt joint with single cover plate.</li>
<li>Butt joint with double cover plate.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-9e863521.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.14 Riveted joint.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-4-5-shrink-fitting">1.4.5 Shrink Fitting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-5-shrink-fitting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Two components can be joined rigidly by shrink fitting. In shrink fitting process, the inner part is made slightly larger in dimension than the hole of the outer part; i.e. with slight interference. This interference can be eliminated for a short time by heating the outer (female) part and the inner (male) part is inserted. While cooling, the outer part shrinks making a strong joint by shrink fitting. The same joint can also be made by cooling the inner part in order to reduce the dimension slightly. Such a fit is some times called expansion fit. To produce a good shrink fit, the parts should be properly designed and machined perfectly to get the required interference at room temperature. The shrink fitting methods are used for lining of heavy shells, fixing of bearing on the shaft, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-4-6-adhesive-bonding">1.4.6 Adhesive bonding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-4-6-adhesive-bonding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Adhesive bonding is a method of joining materials by the use of adhesive between the surfaces to be joined. The type of adhesive used may be glue, cement or similar bonding agents. The process of adhesive bonding includes the following steps:</p>
<ol>
<li>The surface preparation.</li>
<li>Applying the primer.</li>
<li>Applying the adhesive.</li>
<li>Assembling of various components to be joined.</li>
<li>Curing the bonded joint.</li>
</ol>
<p>Depending on the type of adhesive, the curing procedure varies. This</p>
<p>process may include heating, application of pressure, etc. The joint is prepared in various forms based on the requirement. Generally, for better strength more area of contact of the joining pieces is required. The common types of adhesives used are thermoplastic or thermosetting plastics. The thermosetting plastics cannot be re-melted, if once hardened. The adhesives are available as liquid, paste, tape form, film or powder form. Adhesive bonding is successfully used in wood products, plastics, concrete, paper products, etc. Adhesive bonding cannot give good joint strength for metals; therefore it is seldom used for joining metallic parts.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-5-surface-finishing-processes">1.5 Surface Finishing Processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-5-surface-finishing-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The surface finishing processes primarily intended to give good finish or protective coating to metal surfaces. In some cases a negligible amount of metal is removed from the surface. Anyhow, an appreciable amount of dimensional change does not occur. The finishing processes in which a very small layer of metal is removed are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Honing</li>
<li>Lapping</li>
<li>Buffing</li>
<li>Deburring</li>
<li>Sanding</li>
<li>Super finishing</li>
<li>Tumbling</li>
<li>Polishing</li>
</ol>
<p>The surface finishing processes include coating of the metallic surface for finish, decoration or protection. They are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Galvanizing</li>
<li>Metal spraying.</li>
<li>Anodizing</li>
<li>Electro plating.</li>
<li>Inorganic coating</li>
<li>Painting.</li>
</ol>
<p>The above-mentioned finishing operations are applied only towards the end of the shaping processes of a component.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-6-processes-changing-the-material-properties">1.6 Processes Changing the Material Properties<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-6-processes-changing-the-material-properties" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The components of machines and equipment are mainly made up of metals. While shaping them, if the strength of the metal is low at that stage, the production process will be easy. But, after the production of the parts, they have to be converted into their full strength and wear resistance of the required level. This is possible for steel and its alloys by the process called heat treatment. Other processes which change the material properties are cold-working, hot-working and shot-peening (Figure 1.15).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-b99cbfe4.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg">
FIGURE 1.15 Simplified iron-carbon diagram showing the temperature ranges for heat treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-6-1-heat-treatment-of-ferrous-metals">1.6.1 Heat Treatment of Ferrous Metals<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-6-1-heat-treatment-of-ferrous-metals" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Heat treatment of a metal includes the following three stages:</p>
<ol>
<li>Heating the metal to a predetermined temperature.</li>
<li>Holding the metal at that temperature for a specified time so that the structure of the metal becomes uniform throughout the part.</li>
<li>Cooling the metal at a predetermined rate to acquire the required property. The commonly used heat treatment processes applied to steel can be broadly classified into the following categories:</li>
</ol>
<p>(a) Annealing
(b) Normalizing.
(c) Hardening
(d) Tempering.
(e) Case hardening
(f) Surface hardening.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="annealing">Annealing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#annealing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Annealing is the process of heating the metal to a temperature that will remove the distortion of grains of the metal and then cooling at a slower rate so that, at room temperature it has no grain distortion or strain. Depending on the temperature, sustaining time, cooling rate, the composition of metal, etc., the annealing process can be grouped as:</p>
<ol>
<li>Full annealing</li>
<li>Process annealing</li>
<li>Spheroidize annealing</li>
<li>Diffusion annealing</li>
<li>Isothermal annealing</li>
</ol>
<p>Figure 1.15 gives the simplified iron-carbon equilibrium diagram showing the approximate temperature ranges for heat treatment of steel.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="normalizing">Normalizing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#normalizing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The normalizing process consists of heating the workpiece about <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>50</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">50^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span> above its upper critical temperature and then cooling in still air at room temperature. If necessary, the metal may be held at the elevated temperature for a short period. Normalizing differs from annealing in the sense that the holding time at the elevated temperature is very short or zero and the cooling rate is rapid for the process. The normalizing process removes the internal stresses, refines the grains to smaller size and improves the mechanical properties such as impact strength, yield point, ultimate tensile strength, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hardening">Hardening<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hardening" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hardening is a heat treatment process in which the metal is heated about <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>50</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">50^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">C</span></span></span></span></span> above the higher critical point (A3 line) for steels having less than <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>0.8</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">0.8\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">0.8%</span></span></span></span></span> carbon content. For steels having more than <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>0.8</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">0.8\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">0.8%</span></span></span></span></span> carbon content, this range is taken above the lower critical point (A1 line). Then the metal is held at that temperature for sufficient time to change into austenite structure and cooled very rapidly by quenching in a suitable medium like water or oil. This process gives the maximum hardness for the metal, hence suitable for tools, dies, machine parts, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tempering">Tempering<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tempering" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The subsequent heat treatment for reducing the hardness obtained by hardening process is called tempering. In this process the hardened steel is reheated to a temperature below the lower critical point (A1line), retained at that temperature for sufficient time and then cooled slowly to the room temperature. Depending on the holding temperature range, tempering process can be classified as:</p>
<ol>
