<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Copy the given drawing in the work record.</li>
<li>Collect the tools and the blank piece.</li>
<li>Check the size of the blank for its suitability to make the model as per drawing.</li>
<li>Plan the layout of the pieces to be cut as shown in Figure 3.34.</li>
<li>File the edges to form a perfect rectangle so that one side of rectangle AB = 48 mm.</li>
<li>File the flat sides to bring the thickness to 6 mm.</li>
<li>Mark the dimensions of the model as shown in Figure 3.34(a). and punch along the lines.</li>
<li>Cut the shape using hack saw, after giving allowance for filing.</li>
<li>File the edges of square to correct the dimension and geometrical accuracy.</li>
<li>Finish the joint as shown in the Figure 3.34(b).</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="answer-the-following-questions">Answer the following questions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#answer-the-following-questions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="a-fitting-tools">(A) Fitting tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-fitting-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="b-fitting-processes">(B) Fitting processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-fitting-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="models-for-fitting-practice">Models for Fitting Practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#models-for-fitting-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Make the following models, the allotted time for each model is 3 hours:</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Figure 3.35 gives drawing of a V-joint. Copy the figure and make the model using the given MS flat piece.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-a94b121a.jpeg" alt="img-77.jpeg"></p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Make a dove-tail joint of size shown in Figure 3.36, using the MS flat piece. Also prepare a dimensioned neat sketch of the joint.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-246fc6d9.jpeg" alt="img-78.jpeg">
FIGURE 3.35 V-joint.
FIGURE 3.36 Dove-tail joint.</p>

<p>4</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="arc-welding">Arc Welding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#arc-welding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Arc welding is the term generally used to represent shielded metal arc welding, because of its popularity in general engineering works. It is a manual welding process applicable to most of the metals. With the use of suitable electrodes and power supply, joints of good quality can be produced easily on metal parts of thickness ranging from about 2 mm to higher values. The cost of welding is also low compared to other welding processes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-1-principle-of-shielded-metal-arc-welding">4.1 Principle of Shielded Metal Arc Welding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-1-principle-of-shielded-metal-arc-welding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="4-1-1-arc-welding-principle">4.1.1 Arc Welding Principle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-1-1-arc-welding-principle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Shielded metal-arc welding is a manual arc welding process in which the heat required for melting the joining surfaces is generated by maintaining an electric arc between a flux-coated consumable electrode and the base metal. The flux coating burns along with melting of the tip of the electrode and produces a gaseous shield to protect the electric arc, weld puddle and the neighbouring areas from atmospheric contamination. (Figure 4.1). The flux additionally forms a blanket over the molten weld bead in the form of slag which can be removed later by gentle peening.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-cbf9bce2.jpeg" alt="img-86.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.1 Shielded metal arc welding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="4-1-2-power-supply">4.1.2 Power Supply<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-1-2-power-supply" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The electric power used for arc welding process is of low voltage and high current either AC or DC. To convert the standard three-phase line supply to the required form, any one of the following equipment is used.</p>
<ol>
<li>AC Transformer set for A.C. output</li>
<li>DC Motor generator set for D.C. output</li>
<li>DC Transformer-rectifier set for D.C. output</li>
</ol>
<p>The AC transformer sets are commonly used for manual arc welding. The primary side of the transformer is connected to the supply mains and the secondary side to the electrode and the workpiece. Usually welding transformers are designed to have an open circuit voltage between 45 and 90 volts of AC. Drooping voltage (approximately constant current) characteristic of shape as shown in Figure 4.2 is preferred for manual arc welding.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-112bf036.jpeg" alt="img-87.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.2 Current and voltage of power supply.</p>
<p>Direct current supply is obtained either by using a motor generator set or a transformer-rectifier set. DC supply may be of straight polarity (DCSP) or reverse polarity (DCRP) as shown in Figure 4.3. The voltage varies from 45 to 80 and a drooping voltage characteristics is preferred for D.C. also. The polarity selection can improve the welding conditions like welding of metals which are difficult to weld, overhead position of welding, etc. AC supply gives a mixed performance of DCSP and DCRP</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-d4dc46d6.jpeg" alt="img-88.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-c4b8f5f3.jpeg" alt="img-89.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.3 Polarity of electrodes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-1-3-welding-positions">4.1.3 Welding Positions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-1-3-welding-positions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are five recognized positions for welding and are shown in Figure 4.4.</p>
<ol>
<li>Flat or downhand position</li>
<li>Inclined position</li>
<li>Horizontal-vertical position</li>
<li>Vertical position</li>
<li>Overhead position</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-397c2135.jpeg" alt="img-90.jpeg">
(a) Flat (down hand) position</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-504854a5.jpeg" alt="img-91.jpeg">
(b) Inclined position</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-abd0aec7.jpeg" alt="img-92.jpeg">
(c) Horizontal-vertical position</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-327da88f.jpeg" alt="img-93.jpeg">
(d) Vertical position</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-59d5a9d6.jpeg" alt="img-94.jpeg">
(e) Over head position
FIGURE 4.4 Welding positions.</p>
<p>Among the five positions, the flat or down hand position is considered as the easiest position for welding. Overhead position is the most difficult one in welding because the molten metal and slag flow</p>
<p>downward due to gravity. Special electrode coating and use of direct current of suitable polarity can reduce this difficulty in welding. The remaining welding positions are coming in between the two extremes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-2-welding-electrodes">4.2 Welding Electrodes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-2-welding-electrodes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A manual arc welding electrode consists of a metallic wire called core wire which is coated uniformly with flux. Electrodes can be classified into several types according to the wire material, flux coating and dimensions. They are selected depending on the position of welding, base material thickness, base material composition and the type of welding current available.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="core-wire-material">Core wire material<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#core-wire-material" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Mild steel is the most commonly used core material for electrodes. To suit the base metal composition, steels and other metal alloys are also used as electrodes. The coatings are provided according to the requirements.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="electrode-coverings">Electrode coverings<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electrode-coverings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Flux coating over the core wire of an electrode perform the following important functions to produce good welded joints.</p>
<ol>
<li>To strike and maintain the arc.</li>
<li>To produce a protective gaseous shield around the arc.</li>
<li>To produce a slag blanket on the weld metal.</li>
<li>To deoxidize and refine the weld metal.</li>
<li>To add alloying elements to the welded metal.</li>
<li>To concentrate the arc for better penetration.</li>
<li>To prevent spatter of metal and thus to get rounded appearance to the weld bead.</li>
</ol>
<p>The common ingredients of a flux which help in slag formation and metal refining are asbestos, mica, silica, fluorspar, stealite, titanium dioxide, iron oxide, magnesium carbide, calcium carbonate and different aluminas. Iron powder is added for better weld deposition rate. For stabilizing the arc, potassium silicate and potassium titanate are added. Electrodes of different flux compositions are available in the market and they have to be selected as per the directions given on the packet by the manufacturer.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="electrode-size">Electrode size<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electrode-size" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Electrodes are usually produced in lengths of 250, 300, 350 and 450 mm. About 20 mm of core wire length is kept as bare at one end for inserting into the electrode holder to give the power supply. The size of the electrode is measured and designated mainly by the diameter of the core wire. The common size of electrodes (diameter) in mm is given below. The value in bracket shows the standard wire gauge in British system corresponding to the size used.</p>
<p>1.6 mm (16), 2 mm (14), 2.5 mm (12), 3.2 mm (10), 4 mm (8), 5 mm (6), 6 mm (4).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="is-code-for-electrodes">IS code for electrodes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#is-code-for-electrodes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>For convenience of reference, the electrodes are given a code number with the help of which it is possible to identify the type of electrode, the welding position in which it may be used under different current conditions and the weld metal strength. In Indian system, the code number for covered electrodes consists of the prefix letter M to indicate the suitability of the electrode for metal arc welding of mild steel and of low alloy steels. This is followed by six digits. In addition to this, there may be a suffix letter P to indicate that the electrode is suitable for deep penetration in welding. Figure 4.5 shows an example of the marking on a welding rod packet as per BIS. For more details refer IS:815-1956 and IS 814-1963.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>IS: 815-1956 Coding</th><th></th><th>M 325241P</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Specification</td><td>Position</td><td>Current condition</td></tr><tr><td>Ref: IS: 814-1963</td><td>F. H.</td><td>D+A_{85}</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>FIGURE 4.5 Marking on a welding rod packet.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-3-arc-welding-equipment-and-accessories">4.3 Arc Welding Equipment and Accessories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-3-arc-welding-equipment-and-accessories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The equipment and accessories required for manual arc welding other than the electrode are given below:</p>
<ol>
<li>An equipment to provide high current ranging from 100 to 300 or 600 amperes at an open circuit voltage of about 80 to 100 volts. This is obtained either by a welding transformer</li>
</ol>
<p>or a motor generator set connected to the AC power line.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Cables to convey the welding current from the equipment to the electrode holder and workpiece.</li>
<li>Electrode holder to grip the electrode and also to convey the current to the electrode.</li>
<li>Accessories which the welder has to use during and after welding.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-3-1-welding-equipment">4.3.1 Welding Equipment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-3-1-welding-equipment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Figure 4.6 shows the layout of an arc welding unit. The cables (leads) that carry the welding current are very flexible and generally are made of copper or aluminium wire. The cable is made of very fine and flexible wires. The number strands may range from 800 to 2500. They are insulated by rubber covering and reinforced by fibre and rubber coating. These leads are connected to the machine mechanically through the cable lug. Figures 4.7(a) and (b) show pictorial views of a welding transformer set and a motor generator set.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-e336274c.jpeg" alt="img-95.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.6 Layout of arc welding equipment.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-e77647b8.jpeg" alt="img-96.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-6b3711be.jpeg" alt="img-97.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.7 Arc welding equipment details.</p>
<p>The welding current in a transformer set is controlled by any one of the following method.</p>
<ol>
<li>Tapped choke method</li>
<li>Moving core method</li>
<li>Moving coil method</li>
<li>Magnetic amplifier method.</li>
</ol>
<p>The tapped choke method is the best and most simple method of control of the current output. Hence, such equipment are commonly used in welding shops.</p>
<p>The electrode holders (stingers) [Figure 4.7(c)] come in a variety of sizes ranging the capacity from 150 to 500 amperes. Figure 4.7(a) shows a popular type of electrode holder, the ground clamp (earthing clamp or connection to the workpiece) that completes the circuit between the electrode and the welding machine [Figure 4.7(e)]. This clamp, connected at one end of the cable [Figure 4.7(d)] is bolted to the work or to the metallic platform on which the work is placed for welding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-3-2-operator-accessories">4.3.2 Operator Accessories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-3-2-operator-accessories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The accessories used by an electric arc welder are given below:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Hand screen (face shield): It is used for protection of operator's eyes and face from arc light and molten metal spatter [Figure 4.8(a)].</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Safety goggles: This is a pair of eye glasses to protect welder's eyes from light and spatter of molten metal, similar to face shield [Figure 4.8(b)].</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Chipping hammer: It is used for removing the slag from the weld [Figure 4.8(c)].</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wire brush: This is used for cleaning the weld [Figure 4.8(d)].</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hand gloves: It is used to protect hand from heat and arc [Figure 4.8(e)]. They are usually made of chrome leather.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Apron: For protecting operator's clothes and body from heat and arc [Figure 4.8(f)]. It is usually made of heat resistant chrome leather.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The tools used in welding shops other than the accessories are fitting tools like hammers, cold chisels, bench vice, files, hack saw, V-block, outside and inside callipers, try square, steel rule, etc. Details about these tools are given in Chapter 3 of this book.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-c8857de2.jpeg" alt="img-98.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.8 Arc welding accessories.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-4-types-of-welds">4.4 Types of Welds<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-4-types-of-welds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The welds made in arc welding can be classified into the following categories:</p>
<ol>
<li>Butt weld: This weld is formed by joining the end surfaces of two plates or rods [Figure 4.9(a)].</li>
<li>Fillet weld: This weld is formed by joining surfaces of plates usually at right angles by filling metal [Figure 4.9(b)].</li>
<li>Slot weld: To join two plates of large area, slots are made on one plate and welding is done inside these slots to fill the gap [Figure 4.9(c)].</li>
<li>Plug weld: This joint is similar to slot weld. Here, the slots are replaced by round holes to form the plugs by welding [Figure 4.9(d)].</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-fa172e14.jpeg" alt="img-99.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.9 Types of welds.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-4-1-types-of-welded-joints">4.4.1 Types of Welded Joints<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-4-1-types-of-welded-joints" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The welded joints usually produced can be classified into the following types:</p>
<ol>
<li>Butt joint [Figure 4.10(a)].</li>
<li>Lap joint [Figure 4.10(b)].</li>
<li>Tee joint [Figure 4.10(c)].</li>
<li>Corner joint [Figure 4.10(d)].</li>
<li>Edge joint [Figure 4.10(e)].</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-3cce49fa.jpeg" alt="img-100.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.10 Types of welded joints.</p>
<p>Among the above five types of joints, the butt weld is used for a butt joint and a fillet weld is used for lap joint, tee joint and corner joint. The butt and fillet welds are commonly used to produce welded structures.</p>
<p>The nomenclature of butt and fillet welds are given in Figure 4.11. The size of a fillet weld is specified by its leg. The size of an equal leg fillet weld is the leg length of the largest isosceles right triangle which can be inscribed within the fillet weld cross section. For unequal leg fillet weld, it is the leg lengths of the largest right angled triangle which can be inscribed within the fillet weld cross section.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-54ad643e.jpeg" alt="img-101.jpeg">
(a) Single V-butt weld</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-20fa3791.jpeg" alt="img-102.jpeg">
(b) Fillet weld
FIGURE 4.11 Nomenclature of weld.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-4-2-edge-preparation">4.4.2 Edge Preparation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-4-2-edge-preparation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Depending on the thickness of plates and the size of joint made, the edges of plates are prepared before welding. Suitable edge preparation ensures proper fusion at the root of the weld and use of minimum weld metal. The edge preparation is required mainly for butt welds of plates having thickness ranging from  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>6</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">6\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  to above. Figure 4.12 gives the edges prepared for butt welds. For small rates of production, hand chipping process can be used. For a large-scale production, gas cutting method is commonly employed. Machining process like shaping or planing can also be used for this purpose.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5079752f.jpeg" alt="img-103.jpeg">
(a) Square butt</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-e592d82f.jpeg" alt="img-104.jpeg">
(b) Single V-butt</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-6bf7602a.jpeg" alt="img-105.jpeg">
(c) Single bevel butt</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-0d830f51.jpeg" alt="img-106.jpeg">
(d) Double V-butt
FIGURE 4.12 Edge preparation for butt welds.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-8fbc9315.jpeg" alt="img-107.jpeg">
(e) Single J-butt</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-b1c4a9f1.jpeg" alt="img-108.jpeg">
(f) Single U-butt</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-4-3-number-and-arrangement-of-runs">4.4.3 Number and Arrangement of Runs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-4-3-number-and-arrangement-of-runs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>For thickness of plates measuring  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>6</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">6\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  and above, usually more than one layer of welding is required to get a butt weld of good quality. Each layer of welding is known as a run of weld. For producing a better joint with less distortion of base metal, the number of runs and electrode size are selected as given in the Figure 4.13. For fillet welds, the number of runs are increased, if the thickness of plate reaches  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  or above. It is to be noted that, for butt welds the back sealing run is to be done in down hand position, in addition to the number of runs marked in the table. All slag and irregularities should be removed before starting the next run of weld.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Plate thickness in mm</th><th>Butt weld</th><th></th><th></th><th>Fillet weld</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td>R</td><td>E</td><td>C</td><td></td><td>R E C</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td></td><td>1</td><td>3.2</td><td>90</td><td></td><td>1 4 160</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td></td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>170</td><td></td><td>1 5 200</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td></td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>170</td><td></td><td>1 5 200</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>1</td><td>5</td><td>200</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td></td><td>1</td><td>5</td><td>200</td><td></td><td>1 5 200</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>1</td><td>6</td><td>320</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td></td><td>1</td><td>5</td><td>200</td><td></td><td>3 5 200</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>1</td><td>6</td><td>320</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td></td><td>1</td><td>5</td><td>200</td><td></td><td>1 5 200</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>2</td><td>6</td><td>320</td><td></td><td>2 6 320</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>R=Number of runs, E=Size of electrode in mm,
C=Approximate current in amperes</p>
<p>FIGURE 4.13 Number and arrangement of run in butt and fillet welds.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-5-welding-symbols">4.5 Welding Symbols<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-5-welding-symbols" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A weld can be represented in a drawing by a combination of welding symbols. They are elementary symbols (Figure 4.14) and supplementary symbols (Figure 4.15). The</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Designation</th><th>Illustration</th><th>Symbol</th><th>Designation</th><th>Illustration</th><th>Symbol</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1. Square butt weld</td><td>□</td><td>II</td><td>5. Single J–butt weld</td><td>□</td><td>√</td></tr><tr><td>2. Single V–butt weld</td><td>□</td><td>√</td><td>6. Backing run</td><td>□</td><td>○</td></tr><tr><td>3. Single bevel butt weld</td><td>□</td><td>√</td><td>7. Fillet weld</td><td>□</td><td>△</td></tr><tr><td>4. Single U–butt weld</td><td>□</td><td>√</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>FIGURE 4.14 Elementary welding symbols</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Shape of weld surface</th><th>Symbol</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1. Flush surface</td><td>—</td></tr><tr><td>2. Convex surface</td><td>□</td></tr><tr><td>3. Concave surface</td><td>√</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>FIGURE 4.15 Supplementary welding symbols.</p>
<p>method of positioning welding symbol on a drawing is shown in Figure 4.16. A leader line to identify the joint, a reference line-1 (horizontal continuous) to show the arrow side symbol and a reference line-2 (horizontal line of short dashes) to show the other side symbol form the layout of the arrow unit. The welding symbols are placed on these horizontal reference lines 1 and 2. If the weld is required on the arrow side of the joint, the symbol for the joint should be placed on the continuous line. If the weld is required on the other side of the joint, the weld symbol should be placed on the dashed line. Figure 4.17 shows examples for the use of symbols. If the weld is of same type on both sides, the symbols can be shown on the continuous reference line symmetrically.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-0d3e4351.jpeg" alt="img-109.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.16 Positioning of welding symbols.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5e2a3199.jpeg" alt="img-110.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.17 Use of wedling symbols.</p>
<p>The dimension of a weld can be written on the left-hand side of the symbol, i.e. before the symbol. The number of welds, length of weld, gap between welds etc. are written on the right-hand side of the symbol.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-6-defects-in-weld">4.6 Defects in Weld<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-6-defects-in-weld" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The common defects seen in the shielded metal arc welding can be classified as dimensional discrepancies and structural discontinuity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="a-dimensional-discrepancies">(a) Dimensional discrepancies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-dimensional-discrepancies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Warpage (distortion)</li>
<li>Incorrect joint preparation</li>
<li>Mismatching</li>
<li>Incorrect welding size and profile</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="b-structural-discontinuity">(b) Structural discontinuity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-structural-discontinuity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>orosity</li>
<li>Slag inclusion</li>
<li>Incomplete fusion</li>
<li>Different forms of cracks</li>
</ol>
<p>In butt welds, the defects in profile are over lap, under size bead, excessive convexity and undercut. In fillet welds, the defects in profile are overlap, excessive convexity excessive concavity and undercut. They are shown in Figure 4.18.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-37debbe7.jpeg" alt="img-111.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.18 Profile defects.</p>
<p>Porosity of weld is caused by impurities in the work metal, contamination of surfaces, excess moisture in electrodes covering, improper arc length, excessive current and too high welding speed. Slag inclusions may result by incomplete de-slagging of previous pass, wide electrode weaving, use of too large size electrode, etc. Gaps from incomplete fusion may occur between weld metal and base metal due to excessive travel speed, bridging, excessive electrode size, insufficient current or poor joint preparation. Undercuts are usually seen due to excessive welding current, arc length and electrode tip weaving speed.</p>
<p>Different types of cracks are seen externally and internally in welds and base metal. They may be longitudinal cracks, transverse cracks, crater cracks, underbead cracks and micro cracks. Figure 4.19 gives the common types of cracks, porosity, slag inclusion, etc., seen in welded joints.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-ffcf1c6d.jpeg" alt="img-112.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.19 Cracks, inclusions and porosity in welds.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-7-welding-practice">4.7 Welding Practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-7-welding-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>To get good results in welding works, it is essential to lay down the correct welding procedure for a particular job. The operator has to follow the correct technique in carrying out the practice. The choice of the procedure and technique for making a particular weld depends on the material, its thickness, the type of work and the mechanical properties required at the joint.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-7-1-welding-procedure">4.7.1 Welding Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-7-1-welding-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The steps to be followed to obtain a good weld are given below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Study the given drawing for welding.</li>
<li>Prepare the edges of parts as per the type of weld.</li>
<li>Select the type of electrode depending on the quality of weld and position of weld required.</li>
<li>Select the size of the electrode suitable to the thickness and the type of weld.</li>
<li>Find the approximate length of run per electrode or the speed of travel. Also fix the number of runs, if a multirun</li>
</ol>
<p>weld is required.</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Finalize the welding sequence.</li>
<li>Assemble the pieces to be welded on the welding table or welding floor.</li>
<li>Fix the earthing clamp to the base metal or to the metal plate of the welding floor.</li>
<li>Select the required voltage and current in the welding machine.</li>
<li>Grip the electrode on the electrode holder and check the welding arc by welding on a waste piece. The arc is to be viewed through the handscreen.</li>
<li>Make the tack welds at intervals on the joint.</li>
<li>Check the alignment of the pieces, after the tack weld.</li>
<li>Make the complete welding from one end to the other.</li>
<li>Remove the slag using the chipping hammer.</li>
<li>Inspect the weld for finding the defects, if any.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-7-2-striking-and-maintaining-the-arc">4.7.2 Striking and Maintaining the Arc<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-7-2-striking-and-maintaining-the-arc" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To strike the electric arc, the work is touched with the electrode and the electrode is quickly withdrawn, a small distance not exceeding that required to maintain the arc under welding conditions. The distance is about 3 mm for medium size electrodes [Figure 4.20(a)]. There is a second method in which a scratching is done with the electrode tip across the plate with a slight circular motion, so that at the bottom of its travel the arc is struck. Further motion of the electrode draws the arc to the required length [Figure 4.20(b)]. The second method is better for a beginner, who is usually in trouble with sticking of the electrode to the plate. If the electrode sticks to the plate, it should be freed with a sharp twist. If this fails, the electrode should be removed from the electrode holder and then from the work by chipping.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-12cb0581.jpeg" alt="img-113.jpeg">
(a) Vertical touching method</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-4677a919.jpeg" alt="img-114.jpeg">
(b) Scratch method</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-dd4eb329.jpeg" alt="img-115.jpeg">
(c) Maintaining the arc
FIGURE 4.20 Striking and maintaining the arc.</p>
<p>After the arc has been struck, it is maintained by uniform continuous movement of the electrode towards the welding point. At the same time the electrode is also progressively advanced or moved in the direction of welding. At the instant of striking the arc, the operator should place the face shield in position and observe the arc through the brown glass. When the arc has been drawn, the electrode should be held at an angle of about 70° to 80° to the plate and then moved slowly and evenly towards the operator Figure 4.19(c). The approximate current rating for different electrode sizes, which can easily maintain the arc are given below.</p>
<p>1.6 mm (45 A) 2.5 mm (90 A) 32 mm (130 A)
4 mm (170 A) 5 mm (200 A) 6 mm (320 A)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-7-3-tack-welding">4.7.3 Tack Welding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-7-3-tack-welding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Tack welding is used to ensure the correct line up to the components to be welded. A tack weld is the smallest weld as a spot or short length weld made by smaller size electrode. The tacks are made in intervals so that the components are kept in alignment and correct position during the full welding. The tacks should be carefully deslagged and if there is any imperfection, that should be removed by chipping and remade.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-7-4-weaving-the-electrode">4.7.4 Weaving the Electrode<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-7-4-weaving-the-electrode" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>While depositing weld metal, it is often desirable to make the width of the deposit wider than that obtained by a string bead. In such cases a weaving motion is applied to the electrode as it is advanced along the line of weld (Figure 4.21). By weaving, it is possible to deposit more metal at a single pass. There are a number of weaving motions which are used according to the position of weld and metal filling rate required.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-328b14fc.jpeg" alt="img-116.jpeg">
FIGURE 4.21 Typical electrode weaving motions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-7-5-safety-precautions">4.7.5 Safety Precautions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-7-5-safety-precautions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The safety precautions to be taken in a welding shop are given below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Avoid keeping inflammable materials in the welding shop.</li>
<li>Use hand screen (face shield) while welding.</li>
<li>Welding gloves should be worn during welding and handling of welded components.</li>
<li>Do not chip or deslag metal unless glasses are worn.</li>
<li>Do not weld while standing on a damp floor.</li>
<li>Switch off the welding machine when it is not in use.</li>
<li>Make sure that welding return leads make good contact, thus improving welding conditions and reducing fire risk.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example">Example<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="materials-required">Materials required<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#materials-required" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Work piece: Mild steel flat of size: 120 × 30 × 6 mm — 2 Nos
Electrode: 10 SWG (3.2 mm) MS electrode — 1 No</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tools-required">Tools required<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tools-required" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="list-of-operations">List of operations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#list-of-operations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Marking,</li>
<li>Sawing,</li>
<li>Chipping,</li>
<li>Rough filing,</li>
<li>Welding,</li>
<li>Deslagging, and</li>
<li>Cleaning.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Copy the given drawing in the work record.</li>
<li>Collect the tools, the blank piece and the electrode.</li>
<li>Check the size of the blank for its suitability to make the model as per drawing.</li>
<li>Chip the edges, file them to form the single V as shown in Figure 4.23(a).</li>
<li>Place the workpieces on the welding table keeping the V upside down and the gap between them as 1 mm for tack welding.</li>
<li>Grip the electrode on the electrode holder and check the earth connection to the table.</li>
<li>Set the output current of transformer as 130 A and switch it on. Check the arc by welding on a waste piece.</li>
<li>Do the tack weld at ends and check the alignment of the pieces.</li>
<li>Complete the backing run welding and remove the slag.</li>
<li>Turn the workpiece upside down and do the first run on welding to fill the V-groove up the half depth.</li>
<li>Remove the slag and do the second run of welding to complete the filling of the V-groove as given in Figure 4.23(b).</li>
<li>Remove the slag and check the dimensional accuracy and also the defects, if any.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-b8261ada.jpeg" alt="img-119.jpeg">
(a) Joint preparation</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-f547e9f2.jpeg" alt="img-120.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5e0f76fb.jpeg" alt="img-121.jpeg">
(b) Finished joint
FIGURE 4.23 Single-V butt joint (Actual shape of joint).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="exercises">EXERCISES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exercises" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="questions-for-viva-voce">Questions for Viva Voce<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#questions-for-viva-voce" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-welding-theory">(A) Welding theory<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-welding-theory" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Explain the principle of shielded metal arc welding.</li>
<li>What are the types of currents used for producing the arc?</li>
<li>Distinguish between DCSP and DCRP.</li>
<li>What is meant by welding position?</li>
<li>What are the functions of flux covering on an electrode.</li>
<li>How an electrode is specified?</li>
<li>What are the types of welds?</li>
<li>Explain the types of joints made in arc welding.</li>
<li>Describe the method of specifying size of a weld.</li>
<li>With the help of sketches explain the edge prepared for butt welding.</li>
<li>What is meant by welding symbol? Where it is used?</li>
<li>What are the types of weld defects seen in welded joints? Explain them briefly.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-welding-practice">(B) Welding practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-welding-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol start="13">
<li>Explain the preparations required before arc welding.</li>
<li>Describe the conduct of welding from the beginning to the end.</li>
<li>How the welding arc is struck and maintained?</li>
<li>Explain the method of weaving the electrode tip to get the required bead size.</li>
<li>What is meant by tack welding? Why it is required?</li>
<li>What are the precautions to be taken while conducting a welding process?</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="models-for-welding-practice">Models for Welding Practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#models-for-welding-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
