<p>5</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sheet-metal-work">Sheet Metal Work<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sheet-metal-work" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>H and working of metal sheets of thickness ranging from 0.31 mm to 1.63 mm (Standard wire gauge 30 to 16) using hand tools and simple machines, is generally considered as sheet metal work. Many engineering components like hoppers, trays, guards, covers, pipes, hoods, bends, boxes, household equipment, steel furniture, etc., are made in small and medium quantities by this process. Sheet metal work basically consists of laying out the pattern by development of surfaces, cutting the sheets, folding and joining them to produce the required three-dimensional shapes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-1-metals-used">5.1 Metals Used<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-1-metals-used" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A metal plate of thickness less than 4 mm is considered as sheet. The size of the sheet is specified by its length, width and thickness in mm. In British system, the thickness of sheet is specified by a number called Standard Wire Gauge (SWG). The commonly used gauge numbers and the equivalent thickness in mm are given below.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>SWG (No.)</th><th>16</th><th>17</th><th>18</th><th>19</th><th>20</th><th>22</th><th>24</th><th>27</th><th>30</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Thickness (mm)</td><td>1.62</td><td>1.42</td><td>1.22</td><td>1.02</td><td>0.91</td><td>0.71</td><td>0.56</td><td>0.42</td><td>0.37</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As the gauge number increases, the thickness decreases. The metals used commonly for sheet metal works are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Black iron (uncoated) sheets</li>
<li>Galvanized iron (GI) sheets</li>
<li>Copper sheets</li>
<li>Aluminium sheets</li>
<li>Tin plates</li>
<li>Stainless steel sheets</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="black-iron-uncoated-sheets">Black iron (uncoated) sheets<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#black-iron-uncoated-sheets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Mild steel sheets rolled to desired thickness are annealed (heating in a furnace to red hot temperature and cooling gradually) to convert them to soft metal. This process gives a blue black appearance to the surface and hence, they are known as black iron sheet. The sheets are used to make components like tanks, trays, covers, pipes, etc., by sheet metal work or welding. Then they are painted with enamel to protect them from corrosion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="galvanized-iron-gi-sheets">Galvanized iron (GI) sheets<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#galvanized-iron-gi-sheets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Iron sheets coated with zinc are known as G.I. sheets. The zinc coating protects the iron sheet from corrosion and gives better appearance. They cannot be welded since they produce toxic fume and residues. Many components which are in contact with water are made using G.I. sheets. The material used for sheet metal work in classrooms is G.I. sheets of thickness about 24 gauge (0.56 mm).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="copper-sheets">Copper sheets<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#copper-sheets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Copper sheets are available in the cold rolled or hot rolled form. Cold rolled copper sheets are commonly used to produce special components like hoods, expansion joints, trays, etc., for chemical processes and for making utensils.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aluminium-sheets">Aluminium sheets<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aluminium-sheets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Aluminium having a very small amount of alloying elements like copper, silicon, manganese, iron, etc., is rolled to form sheets. Aluminium sheets are very well used to make articles such as household appliances, building requirements, trays, hoods, hoppers, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tin-plates">Tin plates<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tin-plates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Iron sheets coated with pure tin are called tin plates. The coating gives bright silvery appearance and corrosion resistance. They are mainly</p>
<p>used to make containers for packed food, oil, ghee, etc. and for making dairy equipment, cans and similar products.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stainless-steel-sheets">Stainless steel sheets<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stainless-steel-sheets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Stainless steel is a metal basically containing iron, nickel, chromium and traces of other metals. It is highly corrosion resistant and can be welded easily. Stainless steel sheets are used similar to G.I. sheets. The areas of application include dairies, food processing units, chemical plants, kitchen utensils, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-2-hand-tools">5.2 Hand Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-hand-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For measuring, marking cutting and forming, various types of hand tools are used in sheet metal work. A list of them is given below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Measuring tools</li>
<li>Marking tools</li>
<li>Cutting tools</li>
<li>Forming tools</li>
<li>Joining tools</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-2-1-measuring-tools">5.2.1 Measuring Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-1-measuring-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following types of tools are commonly used in sheet metal shops to measure the dimensions of work pieces:</p>
<ol>
<li>Steel rule</li>
<li>Vernier calliper</li>
<li>Micrometer</li>
<li>Sheet metal gauge</li>
</ol>
<p>For description and figures regarding the above tools, refer Chapter 3 —Fitting.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-2-2-marking-tools">5.2.2 Marking Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-2-marking-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In order to mark the patterns and their details on sheet metal, the following marking tools are generally used.</p>
<ol>
<li>Straight edge</li>
<li>Steel square</li>
<li>Scriber</li>
<li>Divider</li>
<li>Centre punch</li>
</ol>
<p>For marking the straight lines on sheet metal, straight edges [Figure 5.1(a)] are used. The use of steel square [Figure 5.1(b)] is similar to that of try square in bench work. The scribers, dividers, and punches are described with figures in Chapter 3.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-8215271f.jpeg" alt="img-131.jpeg">
(b) Steel square
FIGURE 5.1 Tools to mark straight line and rectangle.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-2-3-cutting-tools">5.2.3 Cutting Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-3-cutting-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To cut the sheet metal as per the pattern drawn and to make holes for rivets etc., the following types of tools are used.</p>
<ol>
<li>Straight snips (shears)</li>
<li>Bent snips (shears)</li>
<li>Solid punches</li>
<li>Hollow punches</li>
<li>Cold chisels</li>
<li>Files</li>
</ol>
<p>A snip is a hand shear used to cut thin sheets of gauge size number 20 or above. It works like ordinary scissors. There are several types and sizes of snips available to cut along straight lines or curved lines. Figure 5.2(a) shows a straight snip having straight blades to cut along straight lines. Figure 5.2(b) shows a bent snip having curved blades to cut along curved lines. These snips are used for cutting thin sheets. The heavier types are known as bench shear and block shear. Solid punches are used to make small holes (2.5 to 10 mm) in sheets for riveting and similar works. Hollow punches are used to make large size holes (10 mm or larger) or to cut circular blanks. They are shown in Figures 5.2(c) and (d). To cut thicker sheets, rivets, rods, wires etc., cold chisels are</p>
<p>used. To finish the edges to the required form, files of various shapes are used. The details about files and cold chisels are given in Chapter 3.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-4d269d00.jpeg" alt="img-132.jpeg">
FIGURE 5.2 Cutting tools.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-2-4-forming-tools">5.2.4 Forming Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-4-forming-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Shaping of the sheet metal such as folding, bending, curling, etc., are done by using the following types of forming tools.</p>
<ol>
<li>Stakes</li>
<li>Hammers</li>
<li>Mallet</li>
<li>Hand groovers.</li>
<li>Pliers</li>
<li>Bench vice</li>
</ol>
<p>The types of anvil (support) on which the sheet metal operations are done are called stakes. They are of various shapes and sizes as shown in Figure 5.3. The stakes act as supporting as well as the forming tool for the shaping operations like bending, seaming, cutting, folding etc. The common forms of stakes are mentioned below:</p>
<p>(a) Half moon stake
(b) Beak horn stake
(c) Hatchet stake
(d) Funnel stake
(e) Horse head stake
(f) Convex stake
(g) Square stake</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-70555b7c.jpeg" alt="img-133.jpeg">
FIGURE 5.3 Different types of stakes.</p>
<p>The sheet metal is shaped by hammering or striking with mallet, after keeping the work on suitable form of stake. The hammers used for sheet metal work are (a) Setting hammer, for setting down the edge while making double seam, (b) Raising hammer for forming curved or hollow shape from flat piece, and (c) Riveting hammer for riveting purpose. Mallets are soft hammers used to give soft blows which will not damage the sheet at the same time will shape them. The commonly used types of hammers and mallets are shown in Figure 5.4.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-b4a356a1.jpeg" alt="img-134.jpeg">
FIGURE 5.4 Different types of hammers.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-2-5-joining-tools">5.2.5 Joining Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-5-joining-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The tools exclusively used for making and finishing joints are:</p>
<p>(a) Hand groovers
(b) Rivet set</p>
<p>(c) Soldering irons</p>
<p>Hand groovers are used to flatten and shape joints made in sheet metal. The tool has a groove of required width and depth like a die. This groover is placed over the joint (double hem or lock seam) and hammered from the top of it, to shape the joint as that of the groove (Figure 5.5). For shaping the second head of a rivet, a riveting tool called rivet set is used. This can convert the rough shape of a rivet formed by hammering to a smooth round head by final hammering over the tool.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-23dec6fb.jpeg" alt="img-135.jpeg">
FIGURE 5.5 Hand groover.</p>
<p>A soldering iron consists of a copper block, fixed on an iron rod with a wooden handle. It is made in various shapes and sizes to suit the use as shown in Figure 5.6. The purpose of the copper block is to act as a heat source for melting and spreading the solder (filler metal) at the joining area. The soldering iron (copper) is heated using furnace, blower or by electrical resistance.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-fc0728de.jpeg" alt="img-136.jpeg">
FIGURE 5.6 Forms of soldering iron (copper).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-3-sheet-metal-operations">5.3 Sheet Metal Operations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-sheet-metal-operations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The standard size sheets of required thickness available in the market, are converted to sheet metal products by various hand operations using the sheet metal working tools. The sheet metal operations commonly used are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Laying out of patterns</li>
<li>Cutting or shearing</li>
<li>Bending and forming</li>
<li>Drawing</li>
<li>Edge forming</li>
<li>Joint making</li>
<li>Soldering</li>
<li>Riveting</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-3-1-laying-out-of-patterns">5.3.1 Laying Out of Patterns<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-1-laying-out-of-patterns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The first operation for a sheet metal work is the preparation of the development of the object to be made. The development with the allowances for joints, is called the pattern stretch out of the object to be shaped. The accuracy of the shape of the object mainly depends on the accuracy of geometry of the pattern.</p>
<p>The pattern stretch out may be drawn on a drawing paper and then, after corrections if any, can be transferred to or can be laid directly on the sheet metal by scribing. For repetition works, a template of the pattern can be prepared and the outline of it can be marked on the sheet metal for reducing the time requirement.</p>
<p>To find the development-patterns of objects, basically there are four methods.</p>
<ol>
<li>Parallel line method: Suitable to prisms and cylinders.</li>
<li>Radial line method: For pyramids and cones.</li>
<li>Triangulation method: For transition pieces.</li>
<li>Approximate method: For spherical surfaces.</li>
</ol>
<p>The method of drawing of development of surfaces is explained in books dealing the subject Engineering Graphics. After developing the outer skin of the object to a plane surface, the allowances for joints, edges, etc. are added to the size to get the final pattern for shearing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-3-2-cutting-or-shearing">5.3.2 Cutting or Shearing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-2-cutting-or-shearing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The cutting or shearing operations are conducted to convert the shape of the sheet to that of the pattern. The different types of shearing operations used are mentioned below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Cutting off: To cut along a straight line to get or remove a piece; snips are used for this.</li>
<li>Parting: To cut into two pieces by removing the scrap; snips are used for this.</li>
<li>Blanking: To remove a large area of sheet metal along a closed boundary, generally a large circular shape; large size hollow punches are used for this.</li>
<li>Punching: This is an operation of making small size holes with a solid punch by hammering on it, after keeping the sheet over a die hole of same diameter of the punch.</li>
<li>Notching: This is a process of removing metal to the desired shape from an edge of the sheet. Snips or clod chisels are used for this.</li>
<li>Slitting: This is a process of cutting along a number of parallel lines to form the slits using snips.</li>
<li>Lancing: This is a cutting operation partly across a strip.</li>
<li>Trimming: This is the operation of cutting away the excess metal by snips or chisel.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-3-3-bending-and-forming">5.3.3 Bending and Forming<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-3-bending-and-forming" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The bending and forming operations shape the metal into angles or curvatures. These operations involve stretching of metal on the outer surface, at the same time compressing the metal on the inner side. The bending and forming operations are done by hammering, with mallets or hammers.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-3-4-drawing">5.3.4 Drawing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-4-drawing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is an operation to produce hollow shapes like vessels from flat thin sheets. Usually a drawing punch and a die of correct size are required for this. Drawing operations are successfully done using hand-operated or power-operated machines.</p>
<p>Vessels or shallow trays can be made by a hand drawing process called hollowing. In this process, the metal is beaten into hollow indentation by stretching due to hammering. Similar to hollowing, raising is another process of forming. Here, the metal is beaten over a spherical head using hammers, so that the metal is raised at the middle of a blank forming a spherical projection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-3-5-edge-forming">5.3.5 Edge Forming<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-5-edge-forming" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>For sheet metal objects strength is given to the edge and the sharpness is eliminated by folding the edge. The common types of foldings used in sheet metal work are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Single hem</li>
<li>Double hem</li>
<li>Wired edge</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-2c2f93a8.jpeg" alt="img-137.jpeg">
Figure 5.7 shows the three types of edge foldings. A wired edge consists of an edge wrapped around a steel wire for better strength.
FIGURE 5.7 Types of folded edges.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-3-6-joint-making">5.3.6 Joint Making<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-6-joint-making" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The line of joint on a sheet metal pieces is called seam. The most common types of seams are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Lap seam</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Grooved Seam</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Single seam</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Double seam</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dove tail seam</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Flanged (burred) bottom seam</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Lap seam: This is the simplest seam used in sheet metal work [Figure 5.8(a)]. This consists of one edge lapping over the other and joint is made by soldering or riveting.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Grooved seam: A grooved seam is made by hooking two-folded edges together and then off setting them as shown in Figure 5.8(b). This joint is self-locking and stronger to some extent than lap seam.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Single seam: This seam is used to join a bottom portion to a vertical body as shown in Figure 5.8(c). The bottom edge is hooked over the bent edge of the vertical body. This method of joint can be used for square, rectangular or round containers.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Double seam: This seam is similar to single seam with the difference that the formed edge is bent upwards against the body as shown in</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Figure 5.8(d).</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Dove-tail seam: This seam is used to connect a cylindrical piece to a flat as shown in Figure 5.8(e). The edge of the cylindrical part to be joined is slit at short distance and is bent so that alternate pieces come inside and outside of the joint. Permanent joint is obtained by soldering or riveting.</li>
<li>Flanged (burred) bottom seam: This seam is used to fasten the bottom of a container to its body. The flange of a cylindrical job is often called a burr. The joint consists of a narrow flange which may be joined to inside or outside of the vessel [Figure 5.8(f)].</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-71799dbc.jpeg" alt="img-138.jpeg">
FIGURE 5.8 Types of seam</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-3-7-soldering">5.3.7 Soldering<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-7-soldering" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Soldering is the process of fastening two or more pieces of metal by means of an alloy which has lower melting point than the base metal being joined. To solder sheet metal, the items required are soldering iron (copper), the furnace to heat the soldering iron, solder and a flux. The solder used is of the composition of tin and lead of equal quantity. The flux used is a paste or solution of zinc chloride. After applying the flux to the joining region, heated soldering iron is rubbed over the area and the solder is added to the region to get the joint. For thicker sheets, the joining area is to be heated nearby the soldering temperature using blow lamp or furnace heating.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-3-8-riveting">5.3.8 Riveting<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-3-8-riveting" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Sheet metal objects are joined permanently by riveting. Special rivets called tinner's rivets are used for such purpose. These rivets are made of soft iron and are usually coated with tin. For riveting aluminium or joints of less strength, aluminium rivets are used. Figure 5.9 shows the common shapes of rivets used for sheet metal work.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-71b5ee9c.jpeg" alt="img-139.jpeg">
(a) Tine's' rivet</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-0af9a272.jpeg" alt="img-140.jpeg">
(b) Aluminium rivet</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-7d2b1559.jpeg" alt="img-141.jpeg">
FIGURE 5.9 Types of rivets.</p>
<p>To make a riveted joint, drill or punch the holes of correct size of rivet. The size of rivet is taken as about 2 to 3 times the total thickness of the metal pieces to be joined. Insert the rivet in the hole and set the head on some metal bar or stake. Slip the deep hole of the rivet-set over the rivet tail and strike the rivet-set a few sharp blows. This draws the metal and rivet together. Then blow on the rivet tail to form a rough head using the hammer for riveting. To finish the head, place the cup-shaped rivet-set over the flattened rivet tail and strike the rivet set until the head is shaped.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-4-sheet-metal-work-practice">5.4 Sheet Metal Work Practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-4-sheet-metal-work-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Copy the given drawing in the work record.</li>
<li>Collect the tools, and sheet metal.</li>
<li>Sketch the layout of pattern by drawing the development of the tray and add the allowances for joints as shown in Figure 5.11.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-63edc590.jpeg" alt="img-144.jpeg">
(b) Bottom stretch
FIGURE 5.11 Square tray (layout).</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Mark the layout on the sheet metal using the scriber.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cut the sheet along the marked lines using the snips.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Do all bending operations on the sidepiece to get the square-shaped vertical sides and make the grooved seam using the hand groover.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Place the sidepiece over the bottom stretch out and bend the four edges to form the single seam as given in the drawing.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>File the top edge and the sharp corners to get the finished model.</p>
</li>
</ol>
