<p>8</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="foundry">Foundry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foundry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Founding or casting is the process of forming metal objects by melting the metal and pouring it into mould cavities. A foundry is an establishment for producing castings.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-1-introduction-to-metal-casting-processes">8.1 Introduction to Metal Casting Processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-1-introduction-to-metal-casting-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Metal casting processes can be broadly classified into two types:</p>
<ol>
<li>Conventional sand casting processes</li>
<li>Special casting processes</li>
</ol>
<p>The sand casting process is the most common of all casting processes. Sand moulds are used for this purpose. All the casting processes, other than the conventional sand casting, are called special casting processes. They include shell mould casting, die casting, investment casting, centrifugal casting, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-1-1-sand-casting-process">8.1.1 Sand Casting Process<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-1-1-sand-casting-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The sand casting process consists of basically the making of the sand mould having properly-shaped cavity and pouring molten metal into it to produce a casting. The process of producing sand casting contains the following sections.</p>
<ol>
<li>Pattern making</li>
<li>Moulding</li>
<li>Melting and pouring</li>
<li>Fetling, cleaning and inspection.</li>
</ol>
<p>The shape of a casting is initially made by using wood or plastic which is called pattern. Using the pattern, a cavity is made inside the sand mould and the process is called moulding. Metal to be cast is melted in a furnace and poured to the cavity of the mould. After solidification and cooling, the sand mould is broken and the casting is taken out. Unnecessary projections are removed and the surface is cleaned to finish the casting for inspection.</p>
<p>Depending on the condition of the moulding sand, such as wet or dried at the time of pouring molten metal, the casting processes are divided into two categories:</p>
<ol>
<li>Green (wet) sand mould casting</li>
<li>Dry sand mould casting</li>
</ol>
<p>The metals commonly cast in foundries can be grouped as given below:</p>
<p>(a) Ferrous metals</p>
<ol>
<li>Cast iron</li>
<li>Steels</li>
</ol>
<p>(b) Nonferrous metals</p>
<ol>
<li>Heavy nonferrous metals like copper, zinc, lead and its alloys</li>
<li>Light nonferrous metals like aluminium, and its alloys</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-1-2-sand-mould">8.1.2 Sand Mould<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-1-2-sand-mould" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A sand mould can be described as a void created in compact sand using a pattern, when filled with molten metal it will produce a casting. The process of producing the required shape of cavity is called moulding. Figure 8.1 shows cross section of a sand mould ready for casting. It consists mainly of three parts:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>The lower half called drag resting on a bench or floor</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The upper half called cope, assembled over the drag</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The core fitted inside the cavity of mould</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5be24953.jpeg" alt="img-232.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.1 Flask mould assembly (cross section).</p>
<p>The outer box used for moulding called flask, is in two halves (cope and drag) and is made of iron or wood. The cavity at the centre of the sand mould is shaped by initially placing the pattern and ramming the moulding sand around it. Then the pattern is withdrawn, the core is fitted in position and the two halves (cope and drag) are assembled together to form the mould ready for pouring. Various terms related to the parts of a mould are given in Figure 8.1. The molten metal is poured to the pouring basin, so that it flows to the cavity after passing through the vertical sprue. The metal comes out through the flow-off, when the cavity is filled. Solidified casting is taken out by breaking the mould (Figure 8.2).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-f8d4890c.jpeg" alt="img-233.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.2 Casting.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-2-patterns">8.2 Patterns<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-2-patterns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A pattern is a form made of wood, plastic, metal or any other suitable material, around which moulding sand is packed to shape the casting cavity of a mould. Teak wood is the most commonly used pattern material. Plastics, aluminium, brass and cast iron are also used as materials for pattern in special casting processes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-2-1-types-of-patterns-and-core-boxes">8.2.1 Types of Patterns and Core Boxes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-2-1-types-of-patterns-and-core-boxes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Patterns and core boxes can be classified depending on the shape and size of the casting, number of castings taken and the method of moulding employed. The most commonly used types of wooden patterns for sand casting are given below (Figure 8.3).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-24c161b6.jpeg" alt="img-234.jpeg"></p>
<ol>
<li>Solid pattern</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-064ae799.jpeg" alt="img-235.jpeg">
2. Split pattern</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-1cd81fc2.jpeg" alt="img-236.jpeg">
3. Loose piece pattern</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-bd0b8c9d.jpeg" alt="img-237.jpeg">
4. Match plate pattern</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-54602ecf.jpeg" alt="img-238.jpeg">
5. Gated pattern</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-93a83949.jpeg" alt="img-239.jpeg">
6. Sweep pattern</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5ad6ddc7.jpeg" alt="img-240.jpeg">
7. Segmental pattern</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-6efa8368.jpeg" alt="img-241.jpeg">
8. Follow board pattern</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="figure-8-3-types-of-patterns">FIGURE 8.3 Types of patterns.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#figure-8-3-types-of-patterns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Solid (single piece) pattern: It is a pattern made without joint or parting.</li>
<li>Split pattern: These patterns are split along the parting surface of mould.</li>
<li>Loose piece pattern: This is a pattern with a loose piece so that the pattern can be removed from the mould in pieces after moulding.</li>
<li>Match plate patterns: They are split patterns, the halves of which are separated by a plate of the required thickness and shape. Match plate patterns are generally made of metal or plastic and used for machine moulding of small components.</li>
<li>Gated patterns: The passages for flowing molten metal (gates) are also made using these types of patterns.</li>
<li>Sweep pattern: Moulds which are symmetrical and regular in shape can be made with the use of a wooden piece called "sweep".</li>
<li>Segmental pattern: They are sections of a pattern arranged in such a manner as to form a complete mould by moving from one segment of the mould to the next.</li>
<li>Follow board pattern: Follow board is a wooden board or seat to support the pattern during moulding so that the function of a split pattern is achieved.</li>
<li>Skeleton pattern: When the casting size is too big the pattern is made as wooden frame (skeleton).</li>
<li>Shell pattern: Hollow construction of pattern reduces weight. The inside cavity can be used as a core box in shell pattern.</li>
</ol>
<p>Core boxes, although are not referred to as pattern, are required for producing cores to make holes or undercuts in castings. Core boxes are constructed in wood or metal (Figure 8.4). The most commonly used types of core boxes are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Half core box</li>
<li>Split core box</li>
<li>Dump core box</li>
<li>Strickle core box.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-3c9e32f0.jpeg" alt="img-242.jpeg"></p>
<ol>
<li>Half core box</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-a17fbeec.jpeg" alt="img-243.jpeg">
2. Split core box
3. Dump core box</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-1a1157a4.jpeg" alt="img-244.jpeg">
4. Strickle core box
FIGURE 8.4 Types of core boxes.</p>
<p>While designing the pattern and cores, provision should be made to support the cores rigidly inside the mould cavity. For this, an extension called core print on the core and a similar cavity in the mould are made to support the core.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-2-2-pattern-allowances">8.2.2 Pattern Allowances<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-2-2-pattern-allowances" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The allowances commonly considered for patterns and core boxes are given below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Shrinkage allowances: 9 to 13 mm per metre length (approximately 1%) for cast iron.</li>
<li>Machine finish allowances: About 3 mm is given as extra size for machineing surfaces.</li>
<li>Pattern draft or taper allowances: A taper of 10 to 20 mm per metre (1 in 50) for outside vertical surfaces and 40 to 60 mm per metre (1 in 20) for inside vertical surfaces.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-2-3-pattern-making">8.2.3 Pattern Making<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-2-3-pattern-making" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Basically pattern making is a wood working process. The process is explained in the carpentry section of the book. In pattern finishing, all its angled corners are filled in with fillets. This gives a stronger and cleaner mould and improves strength of the casting. Wooden patterns, after making and finishing, are coated with shellac and painted as per American coding for easy understanding of different parts.</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Black: As cast surface</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Red: Surfaces to be machined</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Yellow: Core prints, and seats</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Red strips on yellow background: Surfaces of loose pieces and their seats.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-3-tools-and-equipment-for-hand-moulding">8.3 Tools and Equipment for Hand Moulding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-3-tools-and-equipment-for-hand-moulding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The tools and equipment used for sand moulding process are grouped as mentioned below:</p>
<p>(a) Sand preparation tools and equipment
(b) Moulding flasks
(c) Moulders tools.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="sand-preparation-tools-and-equipment">Sand preparation tools and equipment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sand-preparation-tools-and-equipment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>To prepare the sand for moulding, the following tools are used (Figure 8.5).</p>
<ol>
<li>Shovel: Used to mix and condition the foundry sand. It is also used to transfer sand from one place to other.</li>
<li>Hand riddle: This is a circular frame fitted with a net, so that the sand can be riddled (sieved) to remove lumps and foreign materials.</li>
<li>Muller: This is a motor operated machine having two stone wheels revolving at slow speed, inside a cylindrical vessel. This can do through mixing of the moulding sand with clay and moisture.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-4624ac91.jpeg" alt="img-245.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.5 Tools for sand preparation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="moulding-flasks">Moulding flasks<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#moulding-flasks" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>They are moulding boxes or flasks used for sand moulding. Wood, steel or cast iron are the materials used for flasks. They can be of closed or open (snap) type. Figure 8.6 shows a wooden flask of closed type. An open type will be hinged at corner and this enables to open the box by side ways. In addition to the drag (bottom part) and the cope (top part), if a third middle box is used for moulding, that box is called cheek.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-a09d2263.jpeg" alt="img-246.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.6 Wooden flask.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="moulder-s-tools">Moulder's tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#moulder-s-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>In hand moulding, a number of tools are used to construct, repair and finish the mould. The most common tools used are given below (Figure 8.7).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-32cae4f7.jpeg" alt="img-247.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.7 Moulders' tools.</p>
<ol>
<li>Moulding board: It is a wooden board to support flask and patterns while moulding.</li>
<li>Rammers: It is used to compact the sand by ramming.</li>
<li>Strike off bar: It is a metal bar to remove excess sand over box.</li>
<li>Vent wire: It is used for making openings in mould called air vents.</li>
<li>Trowels: It is used to finish the mould surface and corners.</li>
<li>Slicks: It is used to repair and finish mould surface and corners.</li>
<li>Lifters or cleaners: It is used to finish vertical surfaces and to remove loose sand from mould.</li>
<li>Sprue and flow off pins: It is used to make the down sprue (the inlet hole for molten metal) and the flow off passages (the outlet of gases and to check the filling of molten metal).</li>
<li>Swab: It is a brush to water the mould surface during finishing or repairing.</li>
<li>Bellows: It is used for blowing off loose sand from mould cavities.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-4-hand-moulding">8.4 Hand Moulding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-4-hand-moulding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Moulding consists of all operations necessary to prepare a mould for receiving molten metal. Sand moulding usually involves packing the mixed moulding sand around the pattern held within the supporting frame (flask), withdrawing the pattern to leave the mould cavity, setting the cores inside the cavity and closing the mould. These operations can be done either by hand moulding or by machine moulding. The most common hand-moulding process is flask moulding done over a work bench (called bench moulding) or on the foundry floor (called floor moulding).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-4-1-moulding-sand-and-its-preparation">8.4.1 Moulding Sand and Its Preparation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-4-1-moulding-sand-and-its-preparation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="composition">Composition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#composition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Moulding sand contains the following ingredients:</p>
<ol>
<li>Sand: Silica sand of average fineness No: 70 is the major content of moulding sand. This comes about 80 to 85% by weight.</li>
<li>Clay: Bentonite is the common type of clay used as binder. The percentage varies from 10 to 15 by weight.</li>
<li>Moisture: Water of about 3 to 6% is added to actuate the clay for developing plasticity and strength.</li>
<li>Additives: For controlling the mould properties, materials like coal dust, cereals, iron oxide, wood flour, etc., are added in small percentages as additives.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="sand-conditioning">Sand conditioning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sand-conditioning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The used moulding sand from the foundry floor is added with sufficient quantity of moisture. This process of spraying water to make it wet is called sand tempering. The tempered sand is sieved using hand operated or mechanically operated sieves to remove iron pieces or any large size particles. New sand and additives are added in sufficient quantity and moisture is added if required. The mixture is then rammed and mixed several times (2 to 4 times) by shawl to coat sand particles with clay. The sand mixture is tested for its quality and is used for moulding, if it is OK. In mechanised foundries muller is used for the mixing operation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="sand-testing">Sand testing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sand-testing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Moulding sand properties like moisture content, permeability, strength, etc., are tested after taking samples from batches of conditioned moulding sand. This is done in sand-testing lab of the foundry. For small foundries only the physical test is conducted by an expert moulder to judge the quality. For this kind of testing, a handful of prepared sand is taken, gripped well with fingers and then released. The sand mass thus produced is broken into two pieces by hand and the edges found at the broken sections are carefully observed. No deformation of edges shows</p>
<p>that the sand conditioning is proper. Gradual setting down of top edge indicates high moisture. Gradual separation of sand grains from the edge shows low moisture and weak bond. By experience, a moulder can judge the quality of the moulding sand by the physical test.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-moulding-sand">Types of moulding sand<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-moulding-sand" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The moulding sand with perfect composition and thorough mixing is used as <em>facing sand</em> (sand near by the pattern) in large size moulds. The rest of the space is filled with lower quality moulding sand and it is called the <em>backing sand</em>. To separate the cope and drag along the mould surface, dry silica sand is used as <em>parting sand</em>. Facing sand with additives added in required proportion is used for cores and is known as <em>core sand</em>.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-4-2-bench-moulding">8.4.2 Bench Moulding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-4-2-bench-moulding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Moulds for making small castings are made in flasks placed over a bench and that is called <em>bench moulding</em>. For medium size casting the same moulding process is followed in foundry floor and that is called <em>floor moulding</em>. Moulding processes using two boxes (the drag and the cope) are called <em>two box moulding</em> and this is the most common type of moulding process. If a third box is used in between the cope and the drag, that box is called <em>cheek</em> and the moulding is called <em>three box moulding</em>. The boxes are fitted with locating and clamping devices in order to keep alignment and to prevent lifting of the cope while pouring molten metal. A two-box moulding process is explained in steps (Figure 8.8).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-bd3ef407.jpeg" alt="img-248.jpeg"></p>
<p>Moulding procedure:</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Place the moulding board on the work bench and keep the pattern over it with its flat surface down.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Set the drag (bottom part of the flask) over the board keeping the upside down, so that the pattern is located approximately at the centre.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Fill about 15 to 20 mm layer of facing sand over the pattern and compact it by hand around the pattern.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Then fill the backing sand in two to three layers followed by a ramming, to fill the drag slightly in excess level.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove the excess sand using the strike-off bar to get a level surface and finish it by using trowel.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Make small pin holes with the help of vent wire, so that permeability is improved for removal of gases during casting.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Turn the drag box upside down, so that the parting line face of the pattern comes to the top.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Finish the top surface with trowel and sprinkle the parting sand over the surface to form a very thin layer for separation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Fit the cope over the drag in alignment and place the top part of the pattern in position.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Place the sprue pin in vertical position on the parting face of mould, nearby the pattern and place the flow off pin on the top most point of the pattern. A layer of facing sand is filled and compacted with hand over pattern for holding the pins in position.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Fill the backing sand in two to three layers followed by ramming so that the cope is filled to a slightly excess level.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove the excess sand using strike off bar and level the surface with a trowel.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Make vent holes using vent wire.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove the sprue and flow off pins carefully and cut the sprue funnel (pouring basin).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Detach the cope from drag and lift it carefully. Place the cope over the board or bench keeping the parting plane upwards.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cut the runner and gate (opening to mould cavity) from sprue hole to the pattern using pointed small trowels, slicks and lift.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Moisture the sand around the pattern with the help of swab and draw out the pattern using a draw screw or spike.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Clean the mould cavity and surface using bellows. Inspect the finish and filling of corners of the cavity. If it is not satisfactory, repair them using moulding tools.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dust the two-halves of mould cavity with graphite or coal powder for better finish of casting.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Assemble the two-halves carefully, after keeping the core in position, and bolt them to keep ready for pouring metal.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>If dry sand moulds are required, the mould halves are dried in sun light or in ovens. They are assembled at the time of pouring of metal.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-5-melting-and-pouring">8.5 Melting and Pouring<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-5-melting-and-pouring" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Metal is melted in furnaces. Commonly used furnaces in foundries can be grouped as mentioned below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Crucible furnaces</li>
<li>Cupola furnace</li>
<li>Electrical furnaces</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="crucible-furnace">Crucible furnace<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#crucible-furnace" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Crucible is a pot placed inside the furnace in which metal is melted, using the heat produced by burning coke, oil or gas. Metal weighing up to 150 kg can be melted in large size crucible furnaces. Figure 8.9 shows schematic diagram of an oil fired tilting type crucible furnace. Usually nonferrous metals are melted using these types of furnaces.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-bcaf4627.jpeg" alt="img-249.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.9 Oil fired tilting furnace.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="cupola-furnace">Cupola furnace<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cupola-furnace" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is the most widely used furnace in small and medium size foundries, suitable for melting cast iron. It is a cylindrical type vertical furnace erected on four legs or columns (Figure 8.10). The shell of cupola is made of 8 to 10 mm thick steel plates and the interior is lined with fire clay bricks. Coke is the material used to burn for producing heat. Pig iron (raw material for cast iron) and pieces of old castings, coke and limestone are charged (filled) from the top side of the furnace in layers. During burning of coke, the metal melts and comes out as molten cast iron through the metal spout. The molten metal is collected in ladles and is poured in moulds.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-39084606.jpeg" alt="img-250.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.10 Copola furnace.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="electrical-furnaces">Electrical furnaces<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electrical-furnaces" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Types of electric furnaces used in foundries are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Induction furnaces: These are suitable for melting all metals and used in large size foundries.</li>
<li>Arc furnaces: These are usually used to melt steel in steel foundries.</li>
<li>Resistance type furnaces: These are usually used to melt nonferrous metals.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="metal-pouring">Metal pouring<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#metal-pouring" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The handling of molten metal from melting furnace to the mould for pouring is done using ladles. Ladles are constructed of steel plates and is coated with refractory material. The large size ladles are handled by overhead travelling cranes. For small and medium size castings, the metal is poured using hand ladles. Figure 8.11 shows a two-men shank ladle used in small foundries.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-64314f65.jpeg" alt="img-251.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.11 Shank ladle for two men.</p>
<p>The molten metal, after tapping from the furnace, should be poured in moulds within seconds. For better fluidity of metal and to get defect-free castings of correct composition, certain chemicals are added to the ladle carrying the molten metal. This process is called inoculation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-6-moulding-practice">8.6 Moulding Practice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-6-moulding-practice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="example">Example<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Construct a sand mould using the wooden pattern given in Figure 8.12.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-2240eaed.jpeg" alt="img-252.jpeg">
FIGURE 8.12 Split pattern of pipe.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-39cfdd8f.jpeg" alt="img-253.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="aim">Aim<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aim" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>To make a sand mould using the given pattern.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="materials-required">Materials required<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#materials-required" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Moulding sand, parting sand, graphite or coal dust, and water.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tools-required">Tools required<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tools-required" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Flask (Cope and Drag), 2. Moulding board, 3. Shovel, 4. Hand rammer, 5. Strike-off bar, 6. Vent wire, 7. Trowel, 8. Slick, 9. Lift, 10. Sprue and flow off pins, 11. Swab, 12. Hand riddle.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="list-of-operations">List of operations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#list-of-operations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Preparation of moulding sand, 2. Compacting of sand over pattern. 3. Withdrawal of pattern, 4. Cutting of gating system, 5. Finishing of the mould surface, and dusting.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="procedure">Procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Copy the given drawing in the work record.</li>
<li>Collect the tools required and the pattern.</li>
<li>Prepare the moulding sand and check its quality by physical test.</li>
<li>Place the moulding board on work bench and set the drag keeping upside down.</li>
<li>Place the pattern at the centre of drag, keeping the parting line touching on the board.</li>
<li>Fill the moulding sand, ram it and remove the excess sand by strike off bar.</li>
<li>Finish the top surface by trowel and make the air vent holes.</li>
<li>Keep the drag box in its normal position, finish the top face with trowel, and springle the parting sand over the surface, after keeping the top half of the pattern above the lower half.</li>
<li>Fit the cope over the drag in alignment and bolt it.</li>
<li>Place the sprue and flow off pins in position and fill the moulding sand after compacting a layer of the sand over the pattern.</li>
<li>Ram the sand with hand rammer and remove the excess sand with strike off bar.</li>
<li>Finish the top surface with trowel and make air vent holes.</li>
<li>Remove the sprue and flow off pins carefully. Detach the cope from drag and place it keeping upside down.</li>
<li>Cut the gating system on the drag surface and withdraw the pattern carefully from the mould.</li>
<li>Clean the mould cavity and dust the surface with graphite or coal powder on the surfaces of the halves. Now the mould is ready for inspection and valuation.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="exercises">EXERCISES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exercises" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<p>9</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="plumbing">Plumbing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plumbing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Plumbing is a skilled trade of working with pipes or tubes and plumbing fixtures. The process is mainly used for the supply of drinking water and the drainage of waste water, sometimes mixed with waste floating materials, in a living or working place. A plumber is someone who installs or repairs piping systems, plumbing fixtures and equipment such as valves, washbasins, water heaters, water closets, etc. Thus it usually refers to a system of pipes and fixtures installed in a building for the distribution of water and the removal of waterborne wastes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="9-1-introduction">9.1 Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9-1-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The Latin word <em>plumbum</em>, means metal lead pipe, is the origin for developing the term plumbing. Plumbing process was originated during the ancient civilizations such as the Greek, Roman, Persian, Indian, and Chinese civilizations as they developed public baths and needed to provide potable water, and drainage of wastes carried by water. Improvement in plumbing systems was very slow, until the 19th century. Eventually, the development of separate underground water and sewage systems eliminated open sewage ditches and cesspools. Most large cities today pump waste water mixed with solid wastes to treatment plants in order to separate and partly purify the water before emptying into streams or other bodies of water.</p>
<p>Plumbing services required in modern buildings and working places can be broadly grouped as:</p>
<ul>
<li>(a) Installation of plumbing systems</li>
<li>(b) Maintenance of the systems</li>
</ul>
<p>These systems can be further classified as conventional and special types:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="conventional-plumbing-systems">Conventional plumbing systems<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conventional-plumbing-systems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Water supply system</li>
<li>Rain water disposal system</li>
<li>Sewage/wastewater disposal system</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="special-plumbing-systems">Special plumbing systems<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#special-plumbing-systems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Hot water supply system</li>
<li>Garden hydrant system</li>
<li>Gas supply system</li>
<li>Solar water heating system</li>
<li>Swimming pool water filtration</li>
<li>Water treatment for domestic use</li>
<li>Fountains, cascades and water bodies</li>
<li>Plumbing for industries</li>
<li>Fire suppression system</li>
</ol>
<p>In the above systems the main items used are pipes, pipe fittings and fixtures. The pipes are cut to the required lengths, and the pipe fittings, sanitary fixtures, etc., are installed in position using different plumbing tools. A student practicing the plumbing process should gather sufficient knowledge about these items and their use.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="9-2-pipes-and-their-joints">9.2 Pipes and Their Joints<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9-2-pipes-and-their-joints" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pipes are manufactured by using different types of materials like steel, cast iron, galvanized iron, brass, copper, aluminum, lead, plastic, concrete, asbestos, etc. They are usually classified according to the material. They are also grouped as cast, welded, seamless, extruded, etc. For conveying large quantity of water, cast iron, steel or concrete pipes having large diameter are usually used. Galvanized iron pipes (GI pipes) are popular for medium and low pressure water supply lines. Plastic pipes are preferred for household uses at low pressure. Pipes are generally specified by their inner diameter (Nominal diameter specified</p>
<p>in inches). Hence, the pipe fitting size is also based on this dimension. But for plastic pipes, this rule is not strictly followed because threading is not usually required for them. For engineering uses, along with the nominal diameter, the pipe thickness is also specified as light, medium or heavy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-pipe-joints">Types of pipe joints<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-pipe-joints" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>According to the pipe material, size and application, different methods are used to join pipes. The most common types of pipe joints are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Screwed pipe joint—for GI pipes</li>
<li>Welded pipe joint—for steel, copper, aluminum and lead pipes</li>
<li>Flanged pipe joint—for cast iron and steel pipes</li>
<li>Soldered pipe joint—for brass and copper tubes</li>
<li>Glued or cemented pipe joint—for PVC pipes</li>
</ol>
<p>Pipes made up of iron (GI pipes) and brass of small and medium diameters (10 mm to 100 mm) are usually joined by screwing the pipe specials with internal or external threads. Welding is used to make permanent joint of medium and large diameter steel pipes. Flanged pipe joints are common in medium and large diameter pipes of cast iron and steel, along with rubber/CAF (Compressed Asbestos Fibre) gaskets. The flanges are screwed to the pipe for smaller diameters but made integral for large diameters. Pipes of copper and brass are usually joined by soldering.</p>
<p>PVC (Poly Vinyl Chloride) pipe is the most popular choice in plastic group. It is rigid, and uses thread or solvent weld (glue) connections. It also can be heat fused. PVC pipes are available in various pressure ratings for water supply and waste water disposal. Standard PVC can only be used for cold water supply, and is a very popular choice for landscape irrigation. The reasons for the popularity are the economy, no corrosion and easiness to work. CPVC is a different type of plastic, which has an extra chlorine atom in the compound, can be used for the hot water supply, and in industry.</p>
<p>To join plastic pipes, gluing or cementing method is used. Solvent cement is the gluing material and it partially melts the surface of the plastic pipe to make the joint. As the glue evaporates within two minutes, a strong joint is obtained.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="9-3-pipe-fittings">9.3 Pipe Fittings<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9-3-pipe-fittings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Screwed pipe fittings, (pipe specials) are removable or temporary pipe connections which permit necessary dismantling or reassembly for the purpose of installation, maintenance, cleaning, repair, etc. The functions of pipe fittings can be broadly classified as:</p>
<ol>
<li>To join two or more pipe lines together</li>
<li>To effect change in diameter or direction</li>
<li>To close the end of a pipe line</li>
</ol>
<p>The most common types of screwed pipe fittings used in galvanized iron (GI) pipe lines and plastic (PVC) pipe lines are shown in Figure 9.1 (1 to 17). A brief description of these fittings is given below.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-2c8585b8.jpeg" alt="img-260.jpeg"></p>
